# Cobol development environment

_422 messages · 47 participants · 2003-06 → 2003-07_

---

### Cobol development environment

- **From:** "Hans Jakschik" <Spectra.Soft@t-online.de>
- **Date:** 2003-06-24T20:23:09+02:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com>`

```
Hi!

I!m looking for a PC-environment which allows (for host-based-applications
i.g.IBM, BULL..)

- to edit a cobol-program
- to compile that program
- to link it
- and to execute it using

-- classical file-organizations (SEQ, REL, ISAM etc..)
-- RDBMS (DB2, Oracle..)

Which toolset (Compiler, RDMBS, GUI...) is adequate?
Price?

Further informations? Links?

Any helpful answer is kindly appreciated.

Hans Jakschik
```

```
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>Further informations? Links?]

www.adtools.com - cheapish
www.acucorp.com - Unknown price (can someone post them? even if
privatelly) + runtime
www.microfocus.com - expensive + runtime

www.liant.com - doesn't create executables (uses runtime), editing
done in WOW and/or Codewatch

IBM VisualAge COBOL
http://www-3.ibm.com/software/awdtools/cobol/va/

Don't know about Realia but the CLC FAQ has the links.
http://www.cobolreport.com/faqs/cobolfaq.htm





Frederico Fonseca
ema il: frederico_fonseca at syssoft-int.com
```

```
news:2h8hfvccktghnte5jhfgqa1he777ib2jj7@4ax.com...
> On Tue, 24 Jun 2003 20:23:09 +0200, "Hans Jakschik"
> <Spectra.Soft@t-online.de> wrote:
>
<snip>
> www.adtools.com - cheapish

Jeez, Fred, I dunno how much you are earning, but I wouldn't say Fujitsu was
"cheap"...<G>

It certainly isn't "cheap" in terms of the facilities available.

It seems to me that there are two de facto COBOL environments for
Client/Server/PC development: (apart from IBM, which is another ball game),
MicroFocus and Fujitsu.

Neither is "cheap". Both are excellent. (MF is disadvantaged by charging for
its run time).

The others are likely to disappear  or be absorbed within 7 years.

The best advice was to direct him to the FAQ.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Using the crystal ball

- **From:** Gisle Forseth <GForseth@acucorp.com>
- **Date:** 2003-07-03T23:07:03-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7BDF3C5E711686498AC6685001541ECE361647@arthur.acucorp.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <2h8hfvccktghnte5jhfgqa1he777ib2jj7@4ax.com> <3ef8fb73_4@news.athenanews.com>`

```
Mr. Dashwood,

I am sorry for being a bit late with this reply, but I've been
travelling.

I just don't understand how you can reason like this, can you please
tell me why you are predicting that one of the most successful COBOL
software company the last decade, currently celebrating its 15 years
anniversary should "disappear" / be absorbed within 7 years?

Wouldn't you consider your foundation a little bit biased, given that
you are a die hard Fujitsu, and before that, what a coincidence, you
happened to use MF?

To bring in a little balance, I could state that Fujitsu and MF would be
gone by 7 years, why not? To me that seems as rational as the opposite.

May I suggest that you desist from making statements that has no root in
reality.

Regards,

Gisle Forseth
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Using the crystal ball

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-07-05T00:22:58+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3f05722c_2@news.athenanews.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <2h8hfvccktghnte5jhfgqa1he777ib2jj7@4ax.com> <3ef8fb73_4@news.athenanews.com> <7BDF3C5E711686498AC6685001541ECE361647@arthur.acucorp.com>`

```

"Gisle Forseth" <GForseth@acucorp.com> wrote in message
news:7BDF3C5E711686498AC6685001541ECE361647@arthur.acucorp.com...
> Mr. Dashwood,
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Glad to...

COBOL is in decline. The reasons for this are manifold. The Market place has
moved on and the methods for developing Computer systems have moved on.

In any diminishing Market there will be a rationalisation of the players.

I predict the winners here will be MicroFocus, Fujitsu, and IBM (it may have
escaped your notice that MicroSoft dropped out of direct involvement with
COBOL some years ago. They pay lip service to support for COBOL as part of
.NET but I have yet to find COBOL code samples on any MSDN sites. I still
have to painstakingly "translate" VB and C samples into COBOL...)

Why these 3? Not because of superior products. (They all have a good
product, as I'm sure AcuCorp does too...). It is because they are the only
ones that have the financial resources to stay the course and the will to do
so. IBM will HAVE to because of the legacy from their mainframe sites, the
other two actually WANT to, they have both invested years in building a
market and responding to changes in it. (BTW, Do AcuCorp offer an Object
Oriented version of COBOL?).

My comment was not intended as a criticism of AcuCorp,


> Wouldn't you consider your foundation a little bit biased, given that
> you are a die hard Fujitsu, and before that, what a coincidence, you
> happened to use MF?
>
Nope. Before that I used IBM, ICL, Burroughs, Univac, NCR, Control Data
Corporation and Honeywell. I made it my business right from the start (1965
for me) to get as much different experience as possible, on as many
platforms as possible, with the object of becoming a consultant. To this day
I have no particular bias for any computer company and your "die hard
Fujitsu" comment is way out of order. I am not retained by Fujitsu in any
capacity and I do not receive inducements from them or any other company to
promote or use their products. I say nice things about Fujitsu because I use
it every day and it is a good product. There is no implicit criticism of
other products. (Now that we have cleared my credentials perhaps you would
clear your own...Like, who do you work for?)


> To bring in a little balance, I could state that Fujitsu and MF would be
> gone by 7 years, why not? To me that seems as rational as the opposite.
>

If that is your opinion, indeed, why not? And, if you expressed such an
opinion I would press you on it, exactly as you have done with me. That's
what forums are for...

> May I suggest that you desist from making statements that has no root in
> reality.
>

(Soooo tempting to digress into a discussion about reality...but, I sense
you are seriously wounded by my comments so I'll keep this serious...).

 1. The statements I make about the future (just like ANY statements made by
ANYBODY about the future) are opinions. I like to think they are informed
opinions, but I'm quite happy to debate them and clarify them for anyone who
disagrees. I arrive at these opinions by observing and thinking. They are
usually extrapolations from observable evidence (I claim no psychic
powers...)

2. If I was to comply with  your suggestion, I would be posting only matters
of verifiable fact. There are a number of people here who do that already,
and do it much better than me.

3. If I didn't post opinion I wouldn't receive mail like yours which debates
it and causes me to think about it again. So, I wouldn't grow much, would I?

4. There are many people in this forum who are happy to debate speculation
and matters of opinion, and the indications are that most people prefer me
to post, even if they don't agree with me. I like that. I believe that
"argument" in the Classical sense (not just pointless bickering or a pissing
contest) is good for all concerned. For that reason I defend vehemently the
right of people with whom I have no agreement whatsoever, to post here.

Now that we've cleared that up I come to YOUR post...

First off, could you please cite some reference, statistics, or data, which
shows AcuCorp to be the "most successful COBOL
software company in the last decade"?

If you can prove this, I shall apologise sincerely (I don't say I'm sorry
when I'm not...) for missing AcuCorp from the list of "survivors".

Please be aware I am NOT "anti" AcuCorp, or any other COBOL software vendor.

I just call them like I see them.

Pete.
```

```
"ShadowFox" <me@email.com> wrote:

>P. S. RM-cobol (liant) bought Micro Focus almost 5 years ago
>Enjoy Bob

Bob:

I'm not sure if this is a "tongue-in-cheek" posting or if I am missing
something, but Liant didn't purchase Micro Focus.

Some years ago, Intersolve and Micro Focus merged (or perhaps it was
Micro Focus which purchased Intersolve).......in any case, the new
corporation was dubbed Merant.

A couple years ago, Micro Focus was divested from Merant and purchased
by a group of San Francisco based venture capitalists.

Micro Focus has operated on it's own ever since.

Perhaps Paul can correct me if I am incorrect in my understanding of
history.

Perhaps you are thinking of one of these other acquisiton events which
took place many years ago:

Micro Focus purchased mbp.

The owner of ACE COBOL purchased Ryan McFarland.  Later on, Ryan
McFarland was sold to Liant.




>
>"Paul Barnett" <paul.barnett@nospam.microfocus.com> wrote in message
```

- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <2h8hfvccktghnte5jhfgqa1he777ib2jj7@4ax.com> <3ef8fb73_4@news.athenanews.com> <7BDF3C5E711686498AC6685001541ECE361647@arthur.acucorp.com> <3f05722c_2@news.athenanews.com> <beueqv$1ns$1@hyperion.microfocus.com> <hlPRa.78932$sY2.35071@rwcrnsc51.ops.asp.att.net> <6itfhvkp0akfncegnugq5ls8c7lb3r31ce@4ax.com>`

```

Bob and Bob...
   The comment about Liant buying Micro Focus was so incorrect that
   I couldn't even bring myself to respond thinking it had to be a
   joke posting.
   But here's the true set of facts... In 1996, Micro Focus acquired
   Intersolv (and for those history buffs out there - Intersolv was
   actually a prior merger of Indexed Technologies who had a application
   design package called Excelerator, and Sage Software who had PVCS,
   and together they had build a COBOL generator known as APS).
   Intersolv was much larger than Micro Focus, but was a bit of a
   falling star at the time. Shortly after the acquisition, the
   Intersolv people somehow got control of the board of directors and
   forced some key people out and took over effectively the entire
   corporation. They decided to name the newly formed company "MERANT".
   All of the Micro Focus founders and many key people were either
   forced out or left on the own in a fairly short period after his...
    Mernant treated COBOL as a dirty word. Last year at COBOL Expo, Ian
    Archibald, who I beleive is the current Micro Focus Vp of
    development publicly apoligized during a keynote address to the
    entire group of attendees and stated that "COBOL has been a dirty
    word at Merant". And he promised that Micro Focus was going to rise
    back up out of the ashes. And I actually wish them very well and
    God's Speed in doing so.
    Regarding the Liant remark there is another bit of history that
    could possibly attribute to such a comment. Sometime before the
    Merant mess, Micro Focus acquired a German company known as MBP
    cobol. I can't remember for sure, but I think they bought MBP from
    Liant - someone please correct me if I'm wrong. I think this
    happened around 1994 or 1995, but I can't really remember...
Thanks,
-Howard
```

```
Ah yes.... the shareholders did take some time to approve things. There
was even a shareholder suit by Intersolv shareholders as I remember hat
delayed things quite a while. In fact Marcello came into Micro Focus in
April of 1996 and wrecked havoc - that's it... And then set in motion
```

```
>     Micro Focus was a great company before he took power, and I believe

At the start of Mr. Gumucio's tenure (April 1996), Micro Focus stock price
was $15. When he left (June 1997), the stock price was $30. The stock
continued to climb under Mr. Waters and hit $57 in May 1998 (just before the
Intersolv acquisition). So Howard, I agree with you, that the result "a few
years later" (as they may "reasonably" be attributed to Mr. Gumucio) was
something very different from the "great" company you were familiar with
just a few short years earlier.

Somehow I doubt that's the picture you had in mind. And somehow, sadly, I
doubt any of these facts will cause you to reflect and revise your picture.
Go on, surprise me.

>     It was
>     reported last year that "Micro Focus is trying to rise from the
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>     down, Gary...?

These "ashes" to which you refer to Howard, were the end-result of Micro
Focus operating as a much maligned business unit within MERANT. This entity
came into being a few months after the Intersolv acquisition and the
replacement of Mr. Waters by Mr. Greenfield. Mr. Greenfield, for whatever
reasons, felt the COBOL business had a limited life and used the revenue as
a "cash cow" to fund other initiatives. Micro Focus operated this way from
early 1999 until it was sold in August 2001.

>     Or are you instead going to try to convince everyone
>     how little I must know and that nothing ever did happen to Micro
```

```
Gary,
    It's rather common business knowledge that new management coming in
    and decimating a company to cut costs will indeed artificially
    inflate the share price for some period of time, and it is my
    opinion that this is exactly what you describe below. Now I do
    agree that in making the Intersolv acquisition, that Micro Focus
    lassoed a large somewhat falling star and the result was being
    dragged along into the abyss, but the bottom line in my humble
    opinion is that while many choose to blame Gary Greenfield for the
    demise of Micro Focus,  I'm afraid, I have to look back a bit
    further and in my opinion feel it was Marcello who set the ultimate
    problems into motion with his lack of knowledge of the COBOL
    business, and the internal alliances he made and by forcing certain
    key individuals out, such as the founder and chairman. By the time
    Mr. Waters took over, I suspect the harm was already too great and
    he faced a monumental task in trying to turn things back around.
    But as far as I'm concerned the harm was already done. And of
    course the board overseeing all of this should have the ultimate
    blame laid upon their shoulders in my opinion. Evidently, (and I
    really don't know the details), they did figure out that Marcello
    was not good for Micor Focus and sent him on his way (at least
    that's what I heard, but again, I wasn't there sitting in those
    discussions). But this is not an uncommon story in Silicon Valley.
    And as I stated previously, I am privy to things you are not
    because of my then position and the fact that I actually worked for
    Marcello for a short time  before I left.
     It was not long after Marcello arrived that there were only a few
     individuals left from the original Micro Focus management team that
     had run the company quite well in my opinion. And the individuals
     left were not the most impressive voices from the bunch either.
     So in any regards, I think we'll agree to disagree. And yes, as I
     stated previously, my time and facts were a bit distorted as I had
     forgetten the exact timeline of the Intersolv acquisition. And I do
     thank you for correcting such in your normally gentle way... :-)
Regards,
-Howard
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ *MY* memories of Micro Focus & the mid-90's (was: Cobol development environment

_(reply depth: 16)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-07-20T14:48:23-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bferm9$tkt$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <2h8hfvccktghnte5jhfgqa1he777ib2jj7@4ax.com> <3ef8fb73_4@news.athenanews.com> <7BDF3C5E711686498AC6685001541ECE361647@arthur.acucorp.com> <3f05722c_2@news.athenanews.com> <beueqv$1ns$1@hyperion.microfocus.com> <hlPRa.78932$sY2.35071@rwcrnsc51.ops.asp.att.net> <6itfhvkp0akfncegnugq5ls8c7lb3r31ce@4ax.com> <3127520.1058553416@dbforums.com> <u23Sa.94476$ye4.66771@sccrnsc01> <3129204.1058593096@dbforums.com> <tNlSa.102746$H17.31678@sccrnsc02> <3130595.1058667147@dbforums.com> <D_nSa.102038$N7.13058@sccrnsc03> <3130819.1058680316@dbforums.com> <7iASa.108977$H17.33032@sccrnsc02>`

```
Deleting all of Gary's and Howard's comments below, let me add my own VERY
personal viewpoint.

First, let me say that I went on "long term disability" in June '96 (and was
officially - but "oddly" - still a semi-employee until June '97).  Any
information I have past that time is TOTALLY hear-say.

June of '96 was the 3rd (or possibly the 4th) of the "Micro Focus " LAY-OFFs
(redundancies in "British") of the '90s.  (There had been another set in the
mid-80's - I think - in the pre "IBM COBOL/2" days).  To me, this indicates
SOMETHING was going on to "require" a "right-sizing" of the company
(matching staff to income) well before any acquisition/merger.

I don't know WHICH "Gary" is involved in this thread - but I have a guess.
If it is the one that I think it is, he would be well aware of the "VisOC"
planning and roll-out "issues" that occurred in the early to mid-90's.

Historically (and some of this pre-dates my time with Micro Focus) there was
a time when MF's majority income was from its "ports" to Unix and other
non-PC environments of its COBOL systems.  At the time that IBM and
Microsoft were still TOGETHER in the original OS/2 development, Micro Focus
was *the* provider of the COBOL/2 product. (Interestingly enough a "stripped
down" version of their own development for incredibly ODD reasons.  The
original IBM COBOL/2 product was a '74, not an '85 Standard compiler).
During the late 80's and early 90's, there was a shift to the majority of
MF's revenue (as I recall) coming from "off-loading" (of IBM development and
POSSIBLY production) COBOL (and a little Assembler and even PL/I) systems.
(Some direct sales, some via IBM and/or Microsoft).

As IBM and Microsoft got more and more "hostile", MF eventually produced a
Microsoft "badged" (and tailored) product - and continued to provide an IBM
product - as well as the "purchased from Micro Focus" products.  (CICS, IMS,
and various DB2 solutions were also ongoing at this time).

There were some "mis-steps" during the early 90's in Micro Focus delivered
products.
  - Anyone remember the LOAD time for the dialog-system generated Character
Workbench?
 - Anyone remember the "bug reports" on Workbench V3.0 (as I recall its
number)?

I know that there are some in CLC who have CUSTOMER recollections of VisOC
and what it did or did not (or was reported to or not reported to) be.
However, it is MY impression that it was a semi-trial balloon to get Micro
Focus "better" targeted at the low-end "individual" (or small site) PC
development market.  (It ALSO included a very new "internal" rework of some
of the base technology, but that wasn't clear to the outside world.)

What *I* saw happening in the early 90's (and this may only reflect my role
in the company) was an ATTEMPT to shift the company from getting its major
revenue from
  - IBM mainframe shops using the PC as a DEVELOPMENT platform
        to
 - Developers (enterprise or individual) moving PRODUCTION to the
"Workstation"

For whatever reason(s) (and I have my own personal view - but others
certainly may disagree with this), this shift was a SERIOUS blow to the
revenue stream of pre-merger/acquisition Micro Focus. (Economic issues in
Japan didn't help the company either - as that had been one of the
previously growing revenue sources.)

Therefore, with the slowing revenue stream along with several "poor" (if not
disastrous) product releases, there were frequent (at least annual)
"management RE-organizations".  Even when new CEO's or "whatever" were NOT
coming in, it became harder and harder to see exactly what the Micro Focus
"vision" was - much less how it was DELIVERING on any vision.  All of this
pre-dated the "merger" that may or may not have been an "acquisition" and
may or may not have involved "inflated" stock prices.

  ***

Both Gary and Howard (and possibly other "visitors" to CLC) may remember
(and even KNOW) things that I don't - but I thought that I would, at least,
express my impression of those "days" -  now long gone.
```

```
Bill,
   Very well stated, I must say. There are a few things left out,
   because as you stated, you were not privy to some things.
   I'll fill in a few of the blanks. COBOL/2 was indeed build for IBM
   for OS/2 and then later licensed to Microsoft. This was effectively a
   $900 product with COBOL workbench at the time  was listed at around
   $4500 as I memeber (plus IMS and CICS optional add-ons at an
   additional $1500). At this time (I believe around 1987...). a small
   company named REALIA had a very fast OS/VS compatible COBOL compiler
   and toolset that was beginning to become a thorn in Micro Focus' side
   and was threatening sales because of it's price differential. It sold
   for around $900. So the strategy adopted at the time was to equip IBM
   and Microsoft (the two largest software "sellers" in the business)
   with a $900 compiler (which actully discounted to under $600 as I
   remember) and let them bury REALIA with this compiler.
But Micro Focus also wanted to protect it high end priced Workbench
product so certain items were not included in the COBOL/2 product. These
were certain critical mainframe emulation features, as I remember.
   And I think it is fair to state that this strategy much have worked,
   as REALIA was acquired later by Pansophic who were then acquired by
   C/A, and while C/A produced a more expensive REALIA Workbench for a
   bit, the emphasis seemed to dissipate in this product line and it is
   pretty much dead now.
   But in keeping some of the "goodies" out of the COBOL/2 compiler
```

```
For Both Bill and Gary's edification, Micro Focus had a total of 3 major
layoffs up until and including the 1996 layoff. The first actually came
in 1985 (April in fact). The stock had gone from 10 pounds to 90 pence
in a very short time because of accounting irregularities. If I remember
correctly, it had to do with booking pre-sales (sales not yet completed)
as revenues or something like this. Approximately 10% of the workforce
was laid off. Then next layoff came in 1995 as I remember and then the
one in 1996. I believe there additional smaller layoffs from 1996
onward, but I can't confirm that. I believe a number of people left
between 1996 and present.
Thanks,
-Howard
```

```
>   design package called Excelerator, and Sage Software who had PVCS,

Excelerator was a Computer Aided Software Engineering (CASE) tool.

And Sage Software (based in Rockville, Maryland) had merged with
Polytron who was the original developer of PVCS (Polytron Version
Control System).

>   and together they had build a COBOL generator known as APS).

Sage Software was already selling the APS COBOL code generator before
the merger......it was not a joint effort.  The integration of the
CASE tool and the application generator may very well have been a
joint effort, but the generator was in existence prior to the merger.

>   Intersolv was much larger than Micro Focus, but was a bit of a
>   falling star at the time. Shortly after the acquisition, the
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>    happened around 1994 or 1995, but I can't really remember...

Howard.....you really didn't read my original reply.....did you....?

Because if you had read my original reply to the poster, you would
have seen this quote as well and not repeated what I had already
posted:

"Perhaps you are thinking of one of these other acquisiton events
which took place many years ago:

Micro Focus purchased mbp."

....and I can indeed correct you on the mbp acquisition as well.  mbp
was indeed a German company, located in Dortmund, but they were never
owned by Liant.  Michael Hicks was the manager of the United States
operation upon acquisition and Chris Glazier was the chief technician
here in the United States.  Chris and I have known each other for
years.

I would appreciate it greatly, if in the future you do not suggest
that I posted a statement, when it was clear that I did not post it.

Thank you.

>Thanks,
>-Howard
…[35 more quoted lines elided]…
>http://www.flexus.com[/url] 


Bob Wolfe
```

```
"SkippyPB" wrote:
> What do you call an amrless, legless man hanging on the wall?

Art.

Groan. Let's list them all now shall we?

http://www.humorhost.com/fjgallery/miscjokes/noarms.shtml
What happened when the man with no arms tried to masturbate?
He was stumped.

What do you call a guy with no arms and no legs in a gully?
Rocky

What do you call a guy with no arms and no legs in a field of marijuana?
Bud

What do you call an electrician with no arms and no legs?
Sparky

What do you call a plumber with no arms and no legs?
Wet

What do you call a cat with no legs?
Dogfood

What do you call a dog with no legs?
It doesn't matter. He won't come when you call him anyway.

What do you call two guys with no arms or legs hanging over your window?
Curt n' Rod

What was the name of the limbless guy that fell in the fire?
Bernie

What do you call a guy with no arms and no legs burried 6 feet under?
Doug

What do you call a guy with no arms and no legs burried 3 feet under?
Douglas

What do you call a man with no arms, no legs and no torso?
Dick

What do you call a legless and armless boy on a baseball team?
First base

What was the name of the limbless guy that was boiled by cannibals?
Stu

What was the name of the limbless girl who was stuck on a femce?
Barb

What was the name of the limbless guy that worked at the soda plant?
Tab

What do you call a guy with no arms and no legs that sits on top of a
podium?
Mike

What do you call a man with no arms and no legs under your car?
Jack

What do you call a guy with no arms and no legs hanging on your wall?
Art

What do you call a guy with no arms and no legs in front of your door?
Matt

Bill

Skip

What do you call a girl with no arms and no legs rolling around on the
beach?
Sandy

What do you call a guy with no arms and no legs in a hole in the ground?
Phil

What do you call a guy with no arms and no legs trying to hold-up a bank?
Rob

What do you call a girl with one leg shorter than the other?
Eileen

What do you call a woman with one leg shorter than the other, married to a
politician?
Tipper

What do you call a girl with no arms and no legs on a dirt road?
Dusty

What do you call a guy with no arms and no legs who left a smudge on your
floor?
Mark

A walk

boat?
Bob
```

#### ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-06-24T14:46:38-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bda9r3$ere$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com>`

```
The following is *not* true for Bull (I don't know for them) but for doing
IBM mainframe (MVS primarily  - although VM and VSE are possible with "some
effort") - the leading vendors are:

 IBM itself, see (VisualAge COBOL):
    http://www-3.ibm.com/software/awdtools/cobol/va/

 Micro Focus (Mainframe Express):
    http://www.microfocus.com/products/mainframeexpress/

 CA (Advantage CA-Realia II Workbench):
    http://www3.ca.com/Solutions/Product.asp?ID=1475

All of the above have SQL (DB/2) emulations/functions, IMS, CICS, and
(except IBM) S/370 (S/390) Assembler and JCL options)

    ***

It is *possible* to use Fujitsu products for this, but that is *not* their
primary target (and they really have NOT kept up with IBM mainframe
developments after VS COBOL II).

Other ANSI/ISO conforming compilers would work with a LOT of effort (and for
limited mainframe development).  Also, some of the "C" and "Java" COBOL
products (e.g. Kobol, OpenCOBOL, PerCOBOL) actually have "ported" versions
to IBM Linux/Unix environments - but would NOT work for "normal" IBM
mainframe off-loading.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com>
- **Date:** 2003-07-03T14:59:23+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3071824.1057244363@dbforums.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <bda9r3$ere$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net>`

```

Bill,
    I must reluctantly and respecfully take exception to your comment
    recomending IBM Visual Age and Micro Focus over Fujitsu -
    particularly the comment thatis is "**possible** to use Fujitsu for
    such, indicating it is less than desirable. Please read the
    COMPUTERWORLD story at the following link:

   http://www.computerworld.com/hardwaretopics/hardware/server/story/0,-
   10801,68844,00.html

   We unplugged a 3090 class MVS mainframe and rolled it out to the
   parking lot after porting ALL of their mainframe apps to Windows NT -
   and that was 3 years ago... Please tell me how many Micro Focus or
   AcuCOBOL customers you know for certain have ever pulled a plug on a
   3090 class mainframe.....? I was at Micro Focus for 12 years and
   never knew of a single customer who did so - Micro Focus customers
   developed MVS COBOL applications primarily to send them back to the
   mainframe for production usage - not to decouple them from the
   mainframe for good. There is a serious difference between Mainframe
   **EMULATION** and **WINDOWS PRODUCTION**, and more people need to
   realize such. It really irks me how hung up on silly issues like
   EBCDIC data emulation when looking to migrate their COBOL to the PC
   environment. Sure, it's possible so use EBCDIC data with some
   compilers, but why?! Nothing else on the PC can use EBCDIC! And there
   is overhead associated with the EBCDIC **EMULATION** as well. If you
   are going to use an application in the Windows environment, then make
   the little or no effort of making sure it is ASCII compatible so you
   can use the wealth of other PC tools and software with it for
   goodness sake.  I have taken 1650 MVS COBOL programs through the
   Fujitsu Compiler in the past week and it works like a champ (as
   usual). The vast majority compile without changes. The few that
   require changes are quite simple. Fujitsu does an excellent job with
   MVS COBOL. The customer had tried an appication under Micro focus and
   it ran 12 times slower they claim. I am working on multiple projects
   at this moment porting large scale MVS COBOL shops to Windows with
   the intent of unplugging mainframes. With all due respect, I would
   use no other product for such than the Fujitsu compiler, and yes it
   is true I am a consultant to Fujitsu, but there are few people in the
   industry with my level of experience doing this and knowing you
   personally, I think you should know better.......  (with all due
   respect of course.. :-)  )
-Howard
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-07-04T02:29:47+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3f04e1d0.102996236@news.optonline.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <bda9r3$ere$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <3071824.1057244363@dbforums.com>`

```
hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com> wrote:

> Please tell me how many Micro Focus or
>   AcuCOBOL customers you know for certain have ever pulled a plug on a
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>   mainframe for good.

During the '90s many organizations, especially US and State Government, ported
their entire mainframe workload to Unix. Micro Focus was the most commonly used
compiler. I've worked on these systems; their mainframe origin shows in the code
style. 

CICS compatibility/emulation is not an important issue. In most cases, CICS was
scrapped and the user interface rewritten in GUI. This gave rise to the phrase
client/server, where client was the new GUI stuff and server was the old
mainframe code. Batch programs required little or no change.

> There is a serious difference between Mainframe
>   **EMULATION** and **WINDOWS PRODUCTION**, and more people need to
>   realize such.... The customer had tried an appication under Micro focus and
>   it ran 12 times slower they claim. 

They were running interpreted .int code rather than native .gnt. When comparing
apples to apples, Micro Focus and Fujitsu run at about the same speed. 

>Originally posted by William M. Klein 

>> It is *possible* to use Fujitsu products for this, but that is
>> *not* their
>> primary target (and they really have NOT kept up with IBM mainframe
>> developments after VS COBOL II).

Neither have mainframe application programmers kept up with developments after
VS COBOL II They're still writing COBOL as it was when their Shop Standard was
written. As a result, compatibility with LE-era extensions is usually not an
issue.
```

- **From:** hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com>

```

Robert and Clark: It  is truly amazing how few mainframe programmers
even know about the ANSI 85 extentions such as Reference Modification,
EVALUATE, and intrinsic functions - even though they may well have been
using the IBM VS COBOL II or later compilers for years. I train
Mainframe Shops on  how to use both Win32 and .NET COBOL, and I always
begin my training with a "review" of this newer syntax and about 9 times
out of ten, the students (many of who are 20+ year mainframe COBOL
programmers) are astounded at the ANSI 85 syntax additions and are
shocked to learn that they could have been using them on the mainframe.
I did a class in April with 13 such programmers in it - and they were
running back to their desks at lunch and during breaks not believing
what I was telling them and trying it out. They were truly astounded. I
don't know exactly what this says, except that COBOL community has done
a very poor job getting its message out I believe...
-Howard

> Robert Wagner wrote:
> > 
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> advantage of the many advances in COBOL since COBOL VS.  Most of them
actually came with VS COBOL II in the IBM mainframe world.
```

```
I'm not certain you should lay the blame on the Cobol community.  That 
attitude runs through many other areas of mainframe life.  

Just off the top of my head you will find similar attitudes in the CICS 
world about anything that wasn't supported in CICS v1.3.  Or in the DASD 
management and allocation you will find people who just don't think that 
newfangled SMS thing can do as good a job as they can.  The list goes 
on...TCP/IP, Java, 1972 style terminals, 24-bit code...

It's a symptom of the mainframe being around for 40ish years without 
requiring any users to expose themselves to any change if they didn't 
want to.  Many don't want to.
```

```
"SkippyPB" <swiegand@neo.NOSPAM.rr.com> wrote in message
news:j2gogv8sk85pbs5r5755miboltd5kv4kl8@4ax.com...
| On Wed, 9 Jul 2003 12:12:19 +1200, "Peter E.C. Dashwood"

| >This is the most profound statement I have seen in CLC for a very long
time.
| >
| >Thanks Joe.
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
| or whatever other than by showing your the best at what you do?

The "best" you can do is write maintainable code.
Sometimes the lowest common denominator drags you down.
You really can't just go off on your own.
If you do something new, present it to the group you are in and see what the
reaction is.
If the consensus is, it's good, stick with it, if not, accept it, and find
an environment where you can practice at the level of competence you are
capable of.

Remember they pay for a system that functions properly.
They don't give points for use of the newest instructions.
If you write programs that never abend due to a program bug, and always
produce the correct results, you are a star.
If you deliver ahead of schedule, and under budget, you are a super star.
If you can do that without keeping up to date on the feature of the
language, you're a F**king Genius.


|If I
| have to put in 10 lines of code in my program and my colleague uses an
| LE Function with 4 lines of code to achieve the same results, who is
| the boss going to give the nod to?  Pretty obvious to me.

It depends on who besides your colleague is familiar with LE.
If nobody else can maintain the LE solution, the guy who wrote the 10 lines
of code will get the "attaboy".

| I think some guys are just lazy and maybe overpaid and too application
| educated to risk losing.  Just my humble observations.  Now if I could
| just find the time to learn all those new Assembler mnemonics.....

Some people just don't have access to the latest Manuals.
(I know they are available on-line, and on CD, but others don't.)
I've installed IBM Bookmanager for several groups I've been in, and the
reaction has always been positive.
```

```
"Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
news:3f0b5e74_5@news.athenanews.com...
>
> "Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> This is the most profound statement I have seen in CLC for a very long
time.
>
> Thanks Joe.
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Warning: the following post contains generalizations.  :-)

Pete, though I don't totally agree with your view of the 'Fortress' because
I believe large companies have generally embraced data base technology and
replaced many (most?) of their old flat file applications,  I have to admit
that the 'Fortress' mentality *does* exist when it comes to their Cobol
code.  I have seen many new programs, even those that access modern data
bases, written as if OS/VS Cobol was still the standard.

I think the reason for this is that there are a lot of COBOL programmers who
don't give a damn about programming either as an art form or as scientific
discipline (take your pick).  They spend their days attending endless
meetings with business users trying to tease out system requirements from
endless discussions that go round and round forever....   At the end of the
each day, they don't sign on to CLC to debate the merits of Sections vs
Paragraphs.  To them, programming is a just a chore that they have to do in
order to get the end result the user wants.

Generally, business programmers are given the following objectives from
their manager (in order of importance):

1.  Don't screw up.
2.  Finish the program on time.
3.  Deliver what the user wants.

From the users, they get the following objectives:

1.  Don't screw up.
2.  Deliver what I want.
3.  Finish the program on time.

I think this breeds a very conservative, risk-averse programmer, for whom
avoidance of error is the primary concern.  Don't tell him/her about
EVALUATE, he/she *knows* that nested IFs work.   (Okay, that's a bad
example, EVALUATE, along with END-IF are two of the more popular 'recent'
language innovations, even among the conservative crowd.  Talk to them about
pointers or intrinsic funtions, though, and their eyes glaze over).
```

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:behjsq$9d9$1@peabody.colorado.edu...
>
> On  9-Jul-2003, "Don Leahy" <don.leahy@nospamwhatever.leacom.ca> wrote:
>
> > I think this breeds a very conservative, risk-averse programmer, for
whom
> > avoidance of error is the primary concern.  Don't tell him/her about
> > EVALUATE, he/she *knows* that nested IFs work.   (Okay, that's a bad
> > example, EVALUATE, along with END-IF are two of the more popular
'recent'
> > language innovations, even among the conservative crowd.  Talk to them
about
> > pointers or intrinsic funtions, though, and their eyes glaze over).
>
> The worst for me is shops where basic principles of structured programs
haven't
> had much inroads.   But even these tend to use date functions.   I find
EVALUATE
> much rarer than END-IF.
>
> I have to admit that I don't see much point in pointers, and haven't used
one in
> a normal business program.   So maybe I'm part of this problem.

I wouldn't say that you're part of the problem at all.

When I first saw COBOL 85, right off the bat I was able to make 3
recommendations:

1.  END-IF should be a shop STANDARD (ie, you *must* use it for all new
development and it *should* be used when making maintenance changes)..
2.  EVALUATE should be a shop Recommendation  (ie, *please* use it...you'll
be glad you did)
3.  POINTERs should be on the 'restricted' list.  (ie, you can use them
*only* under special circumstances)

So far, I haven't regretted any of those recommendations.  The only time
I've needed POINTER was to establish <IMS jargon> PCB addresses via the AIB
ENQY call </IMS jargon>.  (Which falls into the 'special circumstances'
category because the benefits of using AIB far outweigh the 'risk' of
messing with POINTERs).

Having said that, I do believe that all programmers should at least
understand POINTERs, even if they don't use them.
```

```
I once ran into a list of 23 consecutive END-IF statements, and the end of a
monstrous construction.
I would have settled for a period.

At one shop, period-less paragraphs were the rage, too much time was wasted
debugging missing or misplaced END-IF statements.
A period in the middle of a paragraph doesn't hurt.

Scope terminators, and in-line PERFORMs made the language better, but in
some instances the result has been coding that is difficult to maintain.
Those of us who have seen the 30 page statement that is nested 15 levels
deep can attest to it.

Standards and Guidelines should also stress the need to write maintainable
code.
```

```
I think this is good advice. I think that the fact that the default
calling convention in COBOL is "By Reference" meaning that large globs
of data typically are not placed on the stack, Pointers are rarely
needed. I typically only use pointers when interfacing to other language
routines that require such. I also use pointers when  I write systems
level software in COBOL, such as a PC CICS or IMS system....
-Howard

understand POINTERs, even if they don't use them.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** LX-i <LXi0007@Netscape.net>
- **Date:** 2003-07-09T18:49:39-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3F0CAA13.6010607@Netscape.net>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <bda9r3$ere$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <3071824.1057244363@dbforums.com> <3f04e1d0.102996236@news.optonline.com> <3F09B38F.99948E0@istar.ca> <3083120.1057607349@dbforums.com> <joe_zitzelberger-5C7DF0.08470708072003@corp.supernews.com> <3f0b5e74_5@news.athenanews.com> <MGXOa.200$O03.10437@news20.bellglobal.com> <behjsq$9d9$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> The worst for me is shops where basic principles of structured programs haven't
> had much inroads.   But even these tend to use date functions.   I find EVALUATE
> much rarer than END-IF.

I've actually started coding Evaluates for contiguous range checking. 
For example...

Evaluate X When 2 Thru 5
     Perform foo
When Other
     Perform bar
End-Evaluate

...instead of...

If X >= 2 And <= 5
     [or]
If    X >= 2
   And X <= 5
     Perform foo
Else
     Perform bar
End-If

But, it just makes sense that you'd see more Ifs than Evaluates, because 
not all conditions lend themselves to the Evaluate statement.  :)
```

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-07-10T02:08:13+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3f0ca68c.296033479@news.optonline.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <bda9r3$ere$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <3071824.1057244363@dbforums.com> <3f04e1d0.102996236@news.optonline.com> <3F09B38F.99948E0@istar.ca> <3083120.1057607349@dbforums.com> <joe_zitzelberger-5C7DF0.08470708072003@corp.supernews.com> <3f0b5e74_5@news.athenanews.com> <MGXOa.200$O03.10437@news20.bellglobal.com> <behjsq$9d9$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote:

>On  9-Jul-2003, "Don Leahy" <don.leahy@nospamwhatever.leacom.ca> wrote:
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>had much inroads.   But even these tend to use date functions.   I find
EVALUATE
>much rarer than END-IF.
>
>I have to admit that I don't see much point in pointers, and haven't used one
in
>a normal business program.   So maybe I'm part of this problem.

I use pointers heavily .. for one purpose only: linked lists. Here are reasons
why lists are SAFER than tables. 

..Lists are, for practical purposes, unlimited in size. Tables require guessing
the maximum size. When the table overflows, it's usually 3AM. Someone has to
make it bigger and rerun the aborted job. This counts as a black mark.

..In years of heavy use, I have never once accidentally stepped on the wrong
data due to an errant pointer. How can it happen? One doesn't tinker with
pointers. Either they point to the next entry or they are null. Assuming you use
a callable function to free memory and null the pointer to it, you'll not have a
'stale' pointer. 

I *have* seen programs step on the wrong data with subscripts that ran off the
end of the table or contain zero. Many shops turn off bounds checking in the
production version of the program.

..'Bounds checking' for pointers is free and mandatory. It doesn't slow
performance in the way subscripts do. When I say 'bounds checking', I mean
checking for a null pointer. The hardware detects it and kicks you off. It's
unlikely to happen in a tested program anyway, because failing to check for the
end of a list would cause the program to fail in normal use. 

..Lists match the structure of databases; tables match the structure of simpler
files. When loading a subset of data into memory, there is less chance for error
if the database structure is preserved. 

..Insertions and deletions are simpler with lists, involving a few pointer
changes. Insertions in tables are cumbersome if the table must be kept in order.
Deletions in tables are just as cumbersome (to fill the hole) OR require lots of
checking for deleted entries, which can easily be overlooked. 

..List entries can be variable length; table entries cannot. This is handy when
working with words and lines of text. You probably don't write text editors or
compilers, but you might write script-like applications, Tables are the reason
why COBOL words are limited to 30 bytes; other languages place almost no limit
on the size of a word. Variable length is also a benefit when working with
abstract data types, such as in a collection class. You might not know until
execution time what the entries will contain. Even if you do know at compile
time, COBOL provides no handy way to abstract the size of a PICTURE. Yes, you
could do it with TYPEDEF (or even COPY).  Have you ever seen TYPEDEF used?

Speed of lists vs. tables is about the same

The only advantage tables have over lists, that I can see, is the ability to
search them with a binary lookup. If that is a concern, you can do the same as
databases -- build an index tree. Or use tables for the ones that need to be
searchable.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** Paul Robinson <postmaster@paul.washington.dc.us>
- **Date:** 2003-07-14T23:28:49+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3F133BFB.1FC5FC4C@paul.washington.dc.us>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <bda9r3$ere$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <3071824.1057244363@dbforums.com> <3f04e1d0.102996236@news.optonline.com> <3F09B38F.99948E0@istar.ca> <3083120.1057607349@dbforums.com> <joe_zitzelberger-5C7DF0.08470708072003@corp.supernews.com> <3f0b5e74_5@news.athenanews.com> <MGXOa.200$O03.10437@news20.bellglobal.com> <behjsq$9d9$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:

> On  9-Jul-2003, "Don Leahy" <don.leahy@nospamwhatever.leacom.ca> wrote:
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> a normal business program.   So maybe I'm part of this problem.

There are basically two uses for pointers I can think of off hand, either you want
to reference something outside of your program, such as a call to an external
routine that requires your program accept data from elsewhere or access something
elsewhere, or you have some record where you want to have a dynamic number of
elements.

By having an 01 level data record which is instantiated new each time, you can use a
'back' pointer to a previous record and a 'forward' pointer to the next (or just an
original pointer to the top of the list and each record contains a forward pointer
to the next entry.)  This allows you to create the equivalent of dynamic arrays
where you can have an essentially unlimited number of entries.

Most applications can be designed to use static arrays or data files instead of this
feature, and that's what's usually done, which is why you're unlikely to see
pointers used in a COBOL program.  It's just been the habit to use a static array or
a data file.

Now, in Pascal, it is extremely common practice to use a pointer and an instantiated
record each time, you're unlikely to see a large static array or a data file used
for handling that sort of thing.

It's all a matter of  common practice and perhaps taste.  Since COBOL was originally
designed without dynamic storage and pointers, and had extremely good random-access
file system support, most programmers used what they knew and didn't learn new
ideas.  Pascal was originally designed with dynamic storage and pointers, and had
terrible to nonexistent random-access file system support.  Thus you have the
difference in usage methods.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** Joe Zitzelberger <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com>
- **Date:** 2003-07-15T12:10:09-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<joe_zitzelberger-048062.12100915072003@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <bda9r3$ere$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <3071824.1057244363@dbforums.com> <3f04e1d0.102996236@news.optonline.com> <3F09B38F.99948E0@istar.ca> <3083120.1057607349@dbforums.com> <joe_zitzelberger-5C7DF0.08470708072003@corp.supernews.com> <3f0b5e74_5@news.athenanews.com> <MGXOa.200$O03.10437@news20.bellglobal.com> <behjsq$9d9$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3F133BFB.1FC5FC4C@paul.washington.dc.us>`

```
Two very common uses that I see are:

Delimited data.  When dealing with other languages/platforms/etc it is 
not uncommon to get a group/collection/etc of data items with each 
starting at the end of the last.  In some cases you can handle this with 
a very complex ODO and nested complex ODOs -- but it is a lot clearer to 
just say "Set address of my-item-dsect to my-item-dsect-next" and be 
done with it.

Passing and sharing parameters around large run units.  Unless you like 
recompiling hundreds or thousands of programs to add an item to each 
program in a linkage chain, passing an area of pointers allows easy 
additions.

In article <3F133BFB.1FC5FC4C@paul.washington.dc.us>,
 Paul Robinson <postmaster@paul.washington.dc.us> wrote:
> There are basically two uses for pointers I can think of off hand, either you 
> want
…[44 more quoted lines elided]…
> that nobody learns the lessons that history teaches us."
```

```
this term. (It isn't just about resistance to Database technology...). I
have therefore started another thread on Fortress COBOL. If, by discussion
in this group we can arrive at some of the REAL reasons why people adopt
this mentality, I believe we can achieve something that is useful.


> I think the reason for this is that there are a lot of COBOL programmers
who
> don't give a damn about programming either as an art form or as scientific
> discipline (take your pick).  They spend their days attending endless
> meetings with business users trying to tease out system requirements from
> endless discussions that go round and round forever....   At the end of
the
> each day, they don't sign on to CLC to debate the merits of Sections vs
> Paragraphs.  To them, programming is a just a chore that they have to do
in
> order to get the end result the user wants.
>

Yes, I have seen this attitude on various sites, but it is rarely
"universal" in a team.

> Generally, business programmers are given the following objectives from
> their manager (in order of importance):
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>

LOL! This is pretty accurate...

> I think this breeds a very conservative, risk-averse programmer, for whom
> avoidance of error is the primary concern.  Don't tell him/her about
> EVALUATE, he/she *knows* that nested IFs work.   (Okay, that's a bad
> example, EVALUATE, along with END-IF are two of the more popular 'recent'
> language innovations, even among the conservative crowd.  Talk to them
about
> pointers or intrinsic funtions, though, and their eyes glaze over).
>

Good point. In "cultures" where people get blamed for mistakes and it is
natural that the staff will become risk averse.

Pete.
```

```
Joe Zitzelberger <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote

> How about Borland's Codewrite Editor with shell scripts attached to menu 
> items to invoke your favorite command line Cobol compiler?

Yes, I have disliked all IDEs that I have tried.  I use variations on
XTREE (such as XTGOLD, ZTW) or Midnight Commander plus selected
editors such as Semware's TSE and Set Edit plus make.

Most editors today will allow compiles to be done from the menu bar,
but I prefer not to sit staring at a compile running in my edit
window, and get on with other stuff while a separate process works out
what to do and does it.
 
> TCO is less than $100 (like 99.99), but much less with a student license 
> I think.

Plenty of stuff can be found really cheap or free.

> MySQL makes a great RDBMS, TCO is $0.

MySQL has only recently added transactions with InnoDB which now make
it suitable for multi-user business applications. Its main strength
has always been very fast search and retrieval for (eg) Web sites, but
not so hot on multi-user updates as is required for business use. I
mainly use PostgreSQL which is equivalently priced and has been more
of a heavyweight solution.
```

#### ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** "Theo Charalambous" <westleylodge@optusnet.com.au>
- **Date:** 2003-06-28T10:11:54+10:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3efcdd47$0$31276$afc38c87@news.optusnet.com.au>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com>`

```
If you would like tospend money, nothing will beat MF. Here in Australia a
full workbench (we are talking having a"little" mainframe setup on your
computer),will set you back around $20,000AUD

For really dazzling,colorfull, MS-like GUI  applications that follow the MS
environment, and have seemless connectivity to MS development environments
(ie VB etc), Fujitsu may be a better solution.

However be carefull of the runtime and license schemes they offer. I had a
horrible experience with the Fujitsu licensing scheme, where they are not
willing to provide me with an activation key after I rebuilt my PC (in other
words money spent previously went down the drain).

VA COBOL is a good mainframe-like environment for the cost (It is by far the
less expensive product).

Here's what I would base my decision on...
If you are going to build an "inhouse" type application, I believe by far if
money is not an issue go Microfocus way.

If you want to build applications to market, I must admit Fujitsu should be
your choice

If you want to integrate or offload mainframe development to a PC in a
"cheaper" method then go VA COBOL

Kind Rgds

Theo
```

```
compiler for the COBOL language that runs on Linux and Windows.  It
costs as little as $59.95 per developer with no run time costs.  You
get all platforms for one price.  It will have SQL pre-processor
support later this summer that will include MySQL, PostgreSQL and
optionally Oracle, DB2 and ODBC interfaces.

We have just the compiler available for the Eclipse IDE on Linux for
just $39.95.
```

```
Robert with all due respect, your assumptions are both incorrect and not
on target to my original post. The custoer experience a 12 time speed
differential between Fujitsu and Micro Focus most certainly wasn't using
Micro Focus .INT code. They were running large size data file
applications with intensive computations. And one of the largest I/O
shops in the country reported to me that a similar type of application
that was taking 12 hours to run under Micro Focus took 47 minutes under
Fujitsu. Fujitsu has a native Windows 32 compiler that generates real
Windows Object code and creates true Windows .EXE's and .DLL's including
com components and now has a separate .NET compiler integrated into
Visual Stuido. Micro Focus has an abstract machine architecture. A
Native Win32 vs. Abstract machine (.GNT code or not - it's still
interpreted) will typically always outperform - it doesn't take a rocket
scientist to figure this one out..... :-)
     Fujitsu uses the same backend for their Win32 COBOL compiler that
     they use for theirt C++ compiler, The result is high speed and more
     important - stability.
      My original note asked how many 3090 MVS shops have rolled
      mainframes out using Micro Focus - not tiny Government old
      370/4300 shops migrated to UNIX.... I still have yet to hear of a
      single such customer - not one. This is the environment I work in
      daily with Fujitsu COBOL.... And I apologize for getting on my
      high horse a bit - not trying to grandstand - I just got quite
      irritated at my good friend Bill's comments about it maybe "being
      possible" to do this with Fujitsu, when its already been done and
      COMPUTERWORLD itself showcased this fact at their highly respected
      Premier 100 conference where they award the top 100 IT
      professionals on thwe planet. Afriad I didn't see Micro Focus or
      AcuCOBOL or Visual Age ther - nor did I hear mention of any of
      them.... :-)
-Howard
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** "Gael Wilson" <gael.wilson@microfocus.uk.com>
- **Date:** 2003-07-04T14:30:11+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<be3urm$751$1@hyperion.microfocus.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3074858.1057312235@dbforums.com>`

```
I'm sorry Howard but you should get your facts straight before commenting.
GNT code on Micro Focus products is absolutely *NOT* interpreted in any way,
shape or form and it never has been. The GNT code is loaded into memory by
the run-time system but the code contained in the GNT is native machine
code, executed directly by the CPU. And it's worth stating that the compiler
can also generate OBJ code which can then be linked to 'true Windows .EXEs
and .DLLs', just like Fujitsu and no doubt many other compilers.

Only INT code is interpreted. This has always been the case and will remain
so (unless somebody creates hardware that executes INT code !).

I don't have any problem with anybody on this forum saying that compiler X
is better than compiler Y because naturally everybody has an opinion but
don't state something as a fact unless you know for certain that it is true.
```

```
I am sitting here chuckling at the debate regarding a seven year death
prediction for Micro Focus and Fujitsu and et al. Why not 6 years or 8
years... :-).  I remember sitting in a meeting at IIT's Publishing
division (Howard W. Sams) in 1981 where an "informed" IT manager was
beseaching the company to abandon COBOL and claimed that all new COBOL
COBOL development would be dead within 5 years. Yes, I think we all know
that the death of COBOL has been prematurely prdicted by a welath of
"enlightened" individuals. But as I sit here typing, I have the
realization that over half of the lines of programming code in product
today are COBOL. Yes, the number is declining. But let me make my own
educated prediction. As long as there is COBL code around in product,
there will be COBOL compilers on the market and of course vendors
selling such. And I really don't think COBOL itself will be dead in 7
years. In fact, I predict you are going to see the growth of COBOL being
migrated to the Windows environment from mainframes. Ring me back in 7
years and lets see who was correct.... :-)
-Howard
```

```
COBOL is a very good product and could compete in the future markets very
successfully. However, resistance to it by the COBOL community has caused it
to miss the boat.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** "Gael Wilson" <gael.wilson@microfocus.uk.com>
- **Date:** 2003-07-04T17:35:50+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<be49ns$87a$1@hyperion.microfocus.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3075625.1057331897@dbforums.com>`

```
Howard,

I think it will bore most people on the newsgroup if I go into too much
detail, but in simple terms GNT code is a Micro Focus propietary executable
format and therefore it requires a Micro Focus run-time system to load it
and start execution of it because the operating system has no knowledge of
it. There is no interpreted code in GNT code so in that respect I would
contest that it IS 100% native machine code, and the code generator will
produce a machine code disassembly listing if you want to look at it.

The reason why both GNT and OBJ formats exist is mainly historical. They are
produced by the same code generator, have the same optimisations and are
therefore extremely similar in content. Some people, having used GNT for
many years, prefer to continue to do so because they don't want to have to
use system linkers, or perhaps they don't want to repackage their modules as
DLLs. It's all down to choice and we have chosen to continue to give
customers that choice. I don't think that we promote GNT over OBJ or
vice-versa.

As for features only available in GNT code, I think you're showing your age
(which, given that I know what you're talking about means that I am showing
mine too...:-) ). There were differences in the 16-bit days but none that I
can think of  in current products.

Regards,

Gael.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2003-07-04T14:33:35-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0307041333.4799f3d8@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3075625.1057331897@dbforums.com> <be49ns$87a$1@hyperion.microfocus.com> <be4dcs$48i$1@panix1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote

> but in simple terms GNT code is a Micro Focus propietary executable

> I, for one, am not bored... but I am confused.  I cannot see any 
> difference from your description between the 'Micro Focus run-time system' 
> and the old GWBasic interpreter; the operating requires the interpreter to 
> 'load and start execution' of a .BAS file because the operating system 
> 'has no knowledge' of what is in a .BAS file.

While the 'load' part may be quite similar, it is the 'execute' that
is the difference.

A BASIC interpreted program is still primarily in source code format
(possibly with keywords tokenised) and thus the interpreter is what is
'executing' and this picks bytes and words out of the 'program' and
works out what is required.

With .int code this is similar except that the interpreter can
directly operate on each byte code because the 'program' is all
parameterised.  The basic cycle of the interpreter is 'read a byte
from the .int, call routine(bytevalue)'.

With .gnt code the run time loads the program, does some fixups (ie
establishes addresses) and then CALLs the .gnt code.  It is the .gnt
that is then executing, just as machine code in a .EXE does.

In fact MF on DOS/Windows/OS/2 can also create .OBJ and these can be
linked to .EXEs and .DLLs (set by the OMF"" option), but the _content_
of these can still be byte code (.int) or generated machine code
(.gnt) depending on the OPT compiler option.

But then many other language systems can do the same: eg MSVC++ can
output pcode in an .OBJ file that is then linked with an interpreter
and other objects to produce a .EXE or .DLL that contains byte code
for the interpreter to execute.

It doesn't have to be machine code to be a .EXE, nor does it have to
be a .EXE for it to be machine code.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <be4dcs$48i$1@panix1.panix.com> <217e491a.0307041333.4799f3d8@posting.google.com> <be505s$17h$1@panix1.panix.com> <be5jm8$3ca$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3f06c1c5_8@news.athenanews.com> <mDzNa.12350$bD1.999139@news20.bellglobal.com>`

```
I'm astonished at you being astonished, Don <G>.

In my reckless youth I was a system programmer in mainframe environments
before we had such things as linking loaders and JIT compilers.

The format of executables (we referred to them as "core images") was exactly
that. A binary image of what would be in memory.

A linkage Editor made all the necessary connections between object modules
BEFORE they became core images. A loader then did exactly that. Loaded them
and established addressability based on the register set.

This was improved later by the advent of a "Relocating Loader" which could
actually "fit" stuff into a foreground or background partition.

This was superseded by the advent of the "Linking Loader" which took on the
functions of the Linkage Editor and the Loader together.

Today we have mainframe RTSs like LE but all that stuff is after my time.

As my "systems experience" on the Intel architecture has been limited to
writing 8086 Assembler for it under DOS, why would you expect me to know the
intimate details of MicroSoft's Load formats? Yes, I knew there were GNTs
and INTs and I used the OMF function to generate OBJ and link it into an
executable (which required no runtime). A facility I miss with Fujitsu...

However, until the Doc actually raised the point, I had never given much
thought as to exactly what was meant by the GNT nomenclature and I can
understand Doc's puzzlement over load "formats" because I share it. (Maybe
it is a mainframe thing...<G>)

more comments below...

"Donald Tees" <Donald_Tees@sympatico.ca> wrote in message
news:mDzNa.12350$bD1.999139@news20.bellglobal.com...
> "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote in message > Far
from
> bored, I'm fascinated...<G>
> >
> > How can a file of executable code exist in more than one format? Surely
a
> > series of instructions is a series of instructions. It must be a binary
> > image of the machine code, for it to execute (must it not?) I'm puzzled
as
> > to how this can be in different "formats".
> >
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> I'm astonished at you Peter ... surely you realize a loader is more
complex
> than a copy to memory, jump to location zero ...

Yes, I am familiar with the functions of a loader (several in fact, as I
summarised above), however, we are not discussing loaders, we are discussing
executable code. I don't see how THIS can be in different "formats". (I
respect Richard's knowledge enough to know that it probably is, if he says
so, but I'd like to understand it more.)

Which would be the more stupid course of action, Don? To say nothing and
never understand it for risk of looking like an uninformed person and the
possibility of drawing criticism like yours, or simply ask the question and
hope for an explanation?

As I don't care whether people think I know nothing or not, it's a
no-brainer. It may be a "stupid question" to you, but it isn't to me (and I
venture  to suggest that Doc Dwarf will be interested in the reply also...)

> of COURSE binary loadables
> have to be in some format or another ...

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2003-07-05T15:58:44-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0307051458.5c0b4f7e@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <be4dcs$48i$1@panix1.panix.com> <217e491a.0307041333.4799f3d8@posting.google.com> <be505s$17h$1@panix1.panix.com> <be5jm8$3ca$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3f06c1c5_8@news.athenanews.com>`

```
"Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote

> How can a file of executable code exist in more than one format? Surely a
> series of instructions is a series of instructions. It must be a binary
> image of the machine code, for it to execute (must it not?) I'm puzzled as
> to how this can be in different "formats".

(1975) On 8080 style CPUs running CP/M a .COM executable file is just
a binary image that is loaded byte-for-byte starting at address 0x100
into memory. It is loaded by the OS which then 'call's the code at
some fixed point.

(1978) On the same CPU family (8 bit) with MP/M
(multiuser/multitasking) the code needed to be relocatable.  It was
put into a .REL file that had a header pointing to the blocks of code
and to blocks of 'fixups'.  Because the 8080 had fixed addressing
(unlike, say, the 1900 which had an offset register) the addresses in
the code were only relative to the start point.  If the code was
loaded into some place in the memory that did not start at 0x00 then
the address would all be wrong.  The block of fixups were the
addresses within the code where the program was 'wrong' and so the
load process would fixup all these leaving a relocated program in
memory that would run.

A compiler output could be linked to make .COM file for CP/M and to
make a .REL relocatable for MP/M (as long as it doesn't rely on MP/M
features).

(1981) MS-DOS 1.x on 8088 was just a copy of what CP/M did with .COM
files.  This limited the program to one segment of 64Kb max.

(1983) MS-DOS 2.x added .EXE format which had a header and then blocks
of code and data. Each block could be placed in a different 'segment'
of memory and there were fixups required to adjust the segment
addresses into the code.

Later Windows .EXEs have attributes attached to each block of code to
make it sharable or protected or discardable or ...

Similarly a compiler output can be linked as a .COM or a .EXE (as long
as it will fit in 64Kb) or even (given different libraries) as a
CP/M-86 .CMD file or an OS/2 .EXE.

And then there are the various file formats for Unix/Linux such as
COFF and ELF, plus many other OSes such as BeOS, BOS, QNX, TheOS, each
with their own file formats.
```

```
>Richard Plinston  <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
>the MVC!'?

The GNT contains the equivalent of X'25' (Intel rep movsb). After machine
language is loaded and executing, the run-time is not involved unless the
generated code calls a library function therein e.g. file IO, dynamic call.
```

- **From:** SkippyPB <swiegand@neo.NOSPAM.rr.com>

```
On 5 Jul 2003 10:18:51 -0400, docdwarf@panix.com enlightened us:

>In article <be5jm8$3ca$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz>,
>Richard Plinston  <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
…[33 more quoted lines elided]…
>DD

An MVC in hex is actually a D2, but that's irrelevant.  The code, as I
understand the explanations given, is in Intel machine code.  You can
equate that to IBM OS/MVS loadlib module code.  But here's the
difference.  The executable code is not stored in an executable file,
i.e., it is stored in a file format known only to Micro Focus and not
Intel/Windows.  Therefore the Micro Focus run time reads the file and
presents it to the OS is the format it (the OS) understands.  While
this might appear to be an interpretation, it really isn't as the code
is already in an executable format but the file it sits in is not.

Regards,
```

- **Date:** 2003-07-05T15:29:34-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0307051429.72e67a4a@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <be5jm8$3ca$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <be6mob$p5m$1@panix1.panix.com> <hftdgv0t1rnb208too7v05r3e4v82b4b59@4ax.com> <be7619$ga7$1@panix1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote

> This seems to be no different than a .BAS file, as a .BAS file is read by 
> the GWBasic interpreter, which 'reads the file and presents to the OS in 
> the format it (the OS) understands'... but I am missing something 
> exquisitely obvious, no?

It seems so.  The BASIC interpreter does _not_ 'present' anything to
the OS. It is irrelevant what the OS understands.  The interpreter
reads the .BAS file from the disk into memory and shuffles it
appropriately and then treats the program code as a block of data and 
                                                                      
                                                            calls its
own internal routines to do the bidding of the BASIC program by
'interpreting' each byte in the data.
 
> Ahhhh... so sitting inside the file, someplace, is the x88 equivalent of a 
> D2, surrounded by an 'envelope' of MicroFocus proprietary... stuff.  

NO. There is a whole bunch of _all_ the intel code required to do the
whole program and this is within a file that has a header block that
is in proprietry format, and possibly some trailer block that is also
proprietry.  The header and trailer are information about the program
(external references, addresses, fixups) that are required in any
relocatable executable, they just aren't the same as MicroSoft .EXE
format, or Unix COFF or ELF or any number of other formats.

The block(s) of Intel codes within the file are just like blocks of
intel code within any other executable format.

> It 
> would seem to me, then, that 'all ya gotta do' (warning klaxons should be 
> sounding) is strip off the proprietary stuff, leading and trailing, and 
> append what's left over to whatever it is that's used as a 'header' for an 
> x88-architecture executable.

The compiler can do that.  MF has an OMF directive which tells it to
write .OBJ for linking to make a .EXE or .DLL (another 2 proprietry
formats, that just happen to be MS rather than MF) or to write a .GNT.

There is no such thing as 'x86-architecture' executable - they are
_all_ proprietry.  Certainly there are blocks of code within the file
that are just intel executable code, and these will be the same for
each format from the same compiler.

There are many advantages to using MF .gnt format and one is that it
is indepenent of the OS (as long as it is Intel).  In theory a .gnt
should be able to run on OS/2, DOS, Windows, Multiuser-DOS, Unix or
Linux even though each of these have different proprietry executable
file formats.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-07-06T01:56:17+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3f07812a.38899098@news.optonline.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <be5jm8$3ca$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <be6mob$p5m$1@panix1.panix.com> <hftdgv0t1rnb208too7v05r3e4v82b4b59@4ax.com> <be7619$ga7$1@panix1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:

>This seems to be no different than a .BAS file, as a .BAS file is read by 
>the GWBasic interpreter, which 'reads the file and presents to the OS in 
>the format it (the OS) understands'... but I am missing something 
>exquisitely obvious, no?

No. An interpreter doesn't present the program's executable code to the OS. All
the OS sees is the interpreter's code, which is laboriously interpreting the
program. 

>>While
>>this might appear to be an interpretation, it really isn't as the code
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>Might it really be that simple?

There is no such thing as an Intel x86-architecture executable file. Leave Intel
out of it; the format of an .exe is defined by Microsoft. You can read about it
at:
http://www.itee.uq.edu.au/~csmweb/decompilation/new_exe.html

As you'll see, the header deals with multiple data and code segments, and with
'fixups' -- address literals which must be modified by the absolute address of
where the data or code segments are loaded to. The format of an .exe is rather
complicated.

Under 32-bit operating systems (with the possible exception of IBM) a program is
not a single address space. Each data and code segment is allocated separately,
with appropriate permissions. Data cannot be executed; code cannot be read or
written.

The loader allocates these segments and fills them with data before returning
them to the OS as a Task. Windows has a loader and Micro Focus has its own. 

In the early days we had .com format, which *was* a very simple memory image.
Load this into memory and branch to x'100'. It had no headers, no concept of
multiple segments. Command.com is probably the last surviving example. It was
bad practice even in the early days, yet people defended .com over .exe in
newsgroups similar to this. No matter how bad the idea, someone will defend it
in a newsgroup.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2003-07-05T13:18:24-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0307051218.7e40ad3e@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <217e491a.0307041333.4799f3d8@posting.google.com> <be505s$17h$1@panix1.panix.com> <be5jm8$3ca$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <be6mob$p5m$1@panix1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote 

> >It is Intel executable machine code contained in a file of MF proprietry 
> >format.
> 
> Your statement of what GNT code is, then, seems to be at odds with the 
> original assertion which I quoted.

Well, why don't you go and argue with the original asserter ?

A .gnt is proprietry format.  A .gnt is machine code.

It is the file that is proprietry (ie it is not a .exe or .dll
format), not the machine code (which is standard Intel or Sun or ..).
 
> >It gets executed by the Intel processor and is not byte code to be read and 
> >interpreted by the run-time.
> 
> All right, then... let me assume a hypothetical example... but I'll have
> to use specifics from the mainframe with which I am familiar.  

Intel code is not IBM mainframe code nor vice versa.

> A simple
> MOVE can get compiled to what the PMAP... errrr, LISTing shows as an MVC.  
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> of the MVC.

What is a 'load module' ? and why does it know or care what an x'25'
is.  Obviously machine code is a series of bytes which indicate the
function, addresses and sizes, but that is for the CPU to decode not
the 'load module'.

> In the GNT module... is there what is an exact equivalent of an X'25' or
> is there something to which the environment (interpreter) says 'Ahhhh,
> X'GG'... I know to treat the following series of bytes as the arguments of
> the MVC!'?

It is Intel machine code, just like what is in .EXE and .DLLs. It is
executed directly by the CPU.  When the program in the .GNT is running
the CPU's 'instruction pointer' points into the .gnt code.

A MOVE instruction in a .int will be a byte code followed by a series
of parameters that specify which routine to use and the addresses and
sizes.  In a .gnt this will be the actual Intel (or other) machine
code to directly do that MOVE.

(or for complicated stuff it may be a call to run-time routines, just
as a .EXE code will).
```

```
I think you may be wrong.
The Intel chip did provide for packed decimal, don't know if it still does.
The Microsoft macro assembler had a DT (Define Ten) statement that defined
packed decimal. It was a little convoluted using it because the Intel chip
processed one character at a time. I don't remember the exact processing
sequence, but you had to adjust the results after an arithmetic operation
(there was an instruction to do so).
The multiple steps, and the looping through the process to process each
character slowed things down.
```
