```
Howard B. - this is actually a good point. The Fujitsu DATE function (as
in ACCEPT FROM DATE) already returns an 8 digit date and I actually find
myself sometimes having to modify customer programs that have had date
windowing code added to them for 6 digit returns from the mainframe DATE
function to be aware of the 8 digit DATE. In effect, I have to write
code to make it look like the Y2K compliant 8 digit date returned by the
Fujitsu compiler is actually a non-Y2K 6 digit date so their sometimes
complex windowing algorithms work without modification because it's
easier this way..... :-)
```

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-07-08T18:09:05-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<befiuf$gih$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3075625.1057331897@dbforums.com> <bedbck$umi$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3086091.1057677324@dbforums.com>`

```
OK, Howard, ...
  Lets talk about Fujitsu's support of "traditional" (and commonly used) IBM
extensions.  The following is just a list from my LATEST (currently
unresolved) Fujitsu problem report of extensions that Fujitsu does NOT
document as supported.  If you have CURRENT (V7) Fujitsu references for
support of any of these, please give me page references.  (You might also
want to pass them along to the Doug B. that we both know who agrees that
they are NOT currently documented within the Fujitsu product)  (Parenthesis
show which page these extensions SHOULD appear in the V7 LRM *if* Fujitsu
were to support them).  I will be happy to provide a "problem report number"
to any Fujitsu employee who thinks these are currently a supported part of
the product.

1)          Abbreviation ID for IDENTIFICATION (V7 LRM - Page 681)

2)          Comment lines before the Identification Division header (V7
LRM - Page 56)

3)          Special Registers (V7 LRM - Page 26)

a.     ADDRESS OF special Register

b.     LENGTH OF special Register

c.     TALLY special register

d.     WHEN-COMPILED special register

e.     SORT-RETURN special register (Also Page 95)

4)          SET POINTER/ADDRESS OF (V7 - Page 222 SR(14) / Page 418)

5)          GOBACK statement (V7 - Page 62 and elsewhere)

6)          File Extensions

a.     Optionality of word RECORD in the ALTERNATE RECORD KEY clause (V7
LRM - Page 143)

b.     Record and alternate record keys may be (signed) numeric or
numeric-edited (V7 LRM Page 143 SR(3) & Page 157 SR(3))

c.     File Status Key may be defined as Numeric (V7 LRM - Page 148 SR(2))

7)          RECORDING MODE clause (V7 LRM - Page 173 and following)

8)          Specifying data items of usage COMPUTATIONAL-3 or PACKED-DECIMAL
in a NUMERIC class test (V7 LRM - Page 294 & 295)

9)          Comparison of an index-name with an arithmetic expression (V7
LRM - Page 308)

10)      The use of the word EQUAL in place of = in the COMPUTE statement
(V7 LRM - Page 337)

11)        Use of The environment name rather than mnemonic in DISPLAY and
ACCEPT (V7 LRM - Pages 320  & 340)

12)       Compiler directing statements (for listing)  - I don't know where
these belong in the LRM

a.     Eject

b.     Skip1/2/3

c.     Title

13)       Nested COPY statements (V7 LRM - Page 500 SR(10))

14)       SUPPRESS phrase of COPY statement (V7 LRM - Page 498 and
following)





P.S.  There are also a number of NEWER extensions, e.g. Procedure-Pointer,
ACCEPT FROM DAY/DATE YYYYDDD/YYYYMMDD, that were introduced in newer
versions of IBM COBOL and which ARE in common use at sites that *are*
sticking with IBM mainframe for production (not a group that you would be
familiar with - if you currently see mostly sites "porting away" from IBM)


Finally, if you do want to get into a discussion of "porting" applications
away from IBM mainframes, then I would be willing to discuss that.  I agree
that Fujitsu does include SOME advantages over IBM and Micro Focus (and
significant advantages over CA-Realia) for that purpose.  However, AcuCOBOL
has additional advantages (and disadvantages) in that area.
```

```
My point with that list was that those features are NOT currently supported
by Fujitsu - even though they *MAY* function.

Especially IBM mainframe COBOL programmers have been "bit" in the past with
ASSUMING that because a "feature" worked at some time in the past, that it
would continue to work in the future - even though the vendor did NOT
document it as supported.

If you want to understand why this is such an issue to IBM mainframe COBOL
programmers, see:

http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/IGY3MG10/4.1.6

"4.1.6 Undocumented OS/VS COBOL extensions that are not supported"

I would LOVE to see Fujitsu add support for these features (especially if
they really DO think that they are a "part" of their product).  However, as
indicated previously at least SO FAR, Fujitsu has explicitly failed to do
so - even though both documentation and sample programs were provided to
them.

You may be "happy" just moving a program from one compiler to another and
seeing what happens and ASSUMING that it will continue to work that way.  I,
on the other hand, do not believe that it is "safe" to assume a feature is
in a compiler (for the long term) unless the vendor explicitly says so.
```

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-07-09T16:56:01-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bei31o$6b6$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3075625.1057331897@dbforums.com> <bedbck$umi$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3086091.1057677324@dbforums.com> <befiuf$gih$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <3088772.1057715427@dbforums.com> <beg73f$kgb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3091134.1057765699@dbforums.com> <behl0t$a75$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <3092055.1057776714@dbforums.com>`

```
One more time, what I was (am) talking about is NOT moving *any* MVS COBOL
programs into PRODUCTION on PC platforms.  There is *almost* no big deal
about doing a "one time" conversion from one compiler  to another.  The
problem with Fujitsu (from my original and subsequent posts) if you are
TRYING to use it to develop programs that are intended to run in production
on the mainframe.

The "obstacles" are:

1) Fujitsu has NOT kept up with IBM mainframe compilers.  Therefore,
features/functionality that WILL work in IBM mainframe production code
canNOT be developed with Fujitsu.

2) Fujitsu does not CLAIM to provide support for distinguishing what source
code (supported with its compiler) will and will not compile cleanly with
the various IBM mainframe compilers.

3) Fujitsu *does* have software "support" for some IBM extensions/features
that they (so far) have NOT documented that they support.  This means that
such code may OR MAY NOT continue to work in follow-on products/releases.
As I have stated earlier, if you can provide ANY *documented* evidence that
Fujitsu "officially" supports some of these features that HAPPEN to work
today, then please let me know where it is.

    ***

As far as porting programs/applications to run in a Fujitsu "workstation"
environment, I agree that Fujitsu is a MAJOR player in this area.  It does
have SOME problems here (such as different run-time library charging
requirements - depending on which COUNTRY your code will run in - and the
APPARENT "functionally stabilized" nature of its NON-.NET compiler, i.e.
NetCOBOL for .NET appears to be where FUTURE enhancements will be made -
without really providing much in the way of "migration" from such prior
products as PowerCOBOL).
```

```
seems the signal to noise ratio is getting a bit high here <G>.

I have found your posts for the most part interesting and enlightening. It

the perils encountered in doing so. And I was particulary interested in your
previous posts about the power of some of the new servers.

Given your connection to Fujitsu, it is very natural that you will be
sensitive to expressed or implied criticism of the Company or its products.

That is right, proper, and understandable.

I love Fujitsu products and endorse them whenever the occasion warrants it.
But I'm not blind, either.

Bill is concerned about documented facilities and compliance.
(Hey, you've known him long enough to know that he really cares about this
stuff, and he is such a valuable asset to CLC BECAUSE he cares about this
stuff <G>. I know of no other person who has such comprehensive knowledge
and understanding of the COBOL Language and Standards, and respect him for
it. It's kind of "silly" (your word...) for you to get wound up over Bill
being Bill...Besides, accusing him of "spreading misinformation" is like

not officially supported or documented.

Many people here will want to know that.

for it.)

But there are many people who do care. (And who is to say they are "wrong"?

I don't believe Bill is "demeaning" Fujitsu with his post; I DO believe you
```

```
hhinman wrote:
> Sorry Bill, but I think I'll just agree to disagree. The bottom line is
> that the proof is in the pudding.. And I have moved **THOUSANDS** of
> mainframe MVS COBOL programs (and from other platforms as well) into
> **PRODUCTION** on PC platforms with Fujitsu and they have been running
> for years without a hitch - **END OF SUBJECT**... 

He's not debating that - he's saying that you've got no guarantee that 
if your programs use any of these features, you will have continued 
success with future versions.  He's using his past experience to caution 
others, which I think is a good thing.  And, he's absolutely right when 
he says that if a feature isn't documented, you can't count on it being 
there in future versions.

This isn't limited to IBM or Fujitsu - we ran into that on the Unisys 
platform as well.  Unisys -themselves- (and, in turn we) had code 
predicated on behavior that changed in "extended mode" (COBOL 85).  This 
difference in behavior is why any attempts to bring our shop up to COBOL 
85 had failed - and, once I identified this behavior, we were able to 
code around it, and are now completely COBOL 85.

> I don't have a
> **SINGLE** failure - not one...  So you go ahead and continue spreading
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> mistake is akin to getting the "Fonz" to do so.... :-)

Again, he's not questioning your successes - he's merely cautioning 
against assuming that these results will continue, especially if some of 
them are depending on undocumented features.  I hope you're right - that 
it never becomes an issue for you.  But, I can't say that I blame him 
for warning about it.
```

```
>     point out many of these things work fine  and you come back and

The problem with undocumented extensions in a compiler is that there is no 
assurance that they will work the way they are documented in _other_ 
systems, nor that they will even exist in a revision of the compiler.

Putting in a support request on a feature that is undocumented is liable to 
be unsatifactory.
```

```
Richard, I think the problem with all of this is that too many people
get hung up on perfection and symantics and thus do nothing and this is
yet another major contributing factor to the decline of COBOL. People on
one hand talk abotu the importants of standards and such out one side of
their mouth and then get hung up on proprietary extensions available on
some specific platform out the other side of their mouth. It is so very
frustrating to see people afraid to even consider modernizing or
migrating their COBOL applications because of these poorly perceived
fears. The bottom line is if you have a decent COBOL compiler on a
platform (of which their are multiple on Windows), and some minute level
of intelligence  and common sense, they should be able to take just
about any mainframe application and make it work on the PC (with the
potential exception ofent some solutions for this - and there are
already several good CICS solutions). I think one thing you can say
about the COBOL community in general is that COBOL programmers focus on
attacking problems and implementing solutions for business. It's a real
shame that the supporting structure such as the ANSI committees take 15+
years to work on a single new standard while the market is dying as a
result of not having such and vendors are going off implementing their
own solutions. And  so when  I har this jibberish about what's in
someone manual as an excuse for not actually having tried it and then
someone stating warnings about undocumented features, I'm sorry but I
get a bit disgusted. The bottom line is that a good carpenter with a
good hammer and saw and screw drivers can build a good bookcase. the
fact that  his hammer  is curved differently or  lighter than others or
that his saw is a hand saw vs. a fancy arm saw, doesn't negate fromt he
fact that he can use his skills to get the job done with what his has.
And compilers are tools in the same way. And there are always challenges
in migrating applications, but for God's sake - can people concentrate
on "Just Doing It" instead of arguing about every possible pitfall. I
coached children's sports for many years. My favorite saying  I used to
share with them.... "If you don't even try, you've already lost"....
(and no disrespect meant.... :-)  )
Thanks
-Howard
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-07-06T11:32:12+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<be7nbp$3u3$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3078201.1057440539@dbforums.com>`

```
hhinman wrote:

> Well this has turned into a rather amusing discussion, hasn't it..? 
> Is it machine code or not? - What are there two different formats 
> (one > proprietary and one "normal) - but they are the same...? hmmm

Executable files contain more than just 'the code'.  The format of the 
executable code is always the same - it is just strings of intel (or Sun or 
whatever) instructions.  The format of the 'other bits' varies, depending 
on the OS and other factors.

There is no 'normal' format.  All formats are proprietry.  Some are 
proprietry to MS, some to MF, some to Unix, some to others.  It happens 
that if you are using a MS OS then you need to use a MS proprietry format 
for the executable files _or_ provide your own loader.

Examples that provide their own loaders are: Python, Java, Netscape, 
CygWin, MicroFocus.

>    And then there is this question of just what the runtime does
>    floating around. Well the runtime must do something significant
>    because Micro Focus charges customers to deploy it on client

Yes, the run-time does.  It provides the multiuser indexed file system, the 
dynamic loading, memory management, the interpreter for any OPT(0) modules 
(.int) and many routines.

>    like "Let me get this - you (Micro Focus) charge $3000-$6000 for a
>    single COBOL compiler and then you want to hit us up for $60 runtime
>    charges for client machines...??!!  

Let me see what MS does: Charges $several000 for a Windows Server and then 
$60 for a client licence, $several000 for a SQL Server and then $60 for a 
client licence.  And Oracle, how do they charge ?

> And do you realize that we can go
>    buy a C++ or Visual Basic compiler for under $400 with no string
>    attached concerning runtimes....?!!!" 

To get the same functionality from C++ or VB you also need to be running, 
say, a multiuser database system.  This may be, say, SQL Server for which 
you will need client licences.

Of course over the last 20 years there have been changes that mean that 
Cobol ISAM file 'database' is no longer competitive with a RDBMS, 
especially when PostGreSQL can be had for free.
```

```
The construct of the .GNT file contains a framing that is specific to
the operating system. So, if it is for instance a Windows Intel target
platform there would a occur some amount of bytes that frame the
forthwith coming Intel Object Code. Thus by way of example a COBOL
character field move would inject a token identifying the susequent
platform specific obejct code. Presume that INTEL/MOVE is a token to
"frame" the next N bytes of native object code, followed by:

INTEL Object code to prime register CX (ECX or what ever) with the
length of the move, followed either by MOVSB or MOVSW, and then followed
by LOOP/REP ( on CX.)

The IBM 390 equivalent would be for instance  MVC 4(6,R9), 0(R2)

OpCode Length Disp-1 Base-REG-1x Disp-2 Base-REG-2x
D2         5         4         Register 9     0         Register 2
OR in Object Code Format
D2 05 9 004 2 000

This .GNT construct adds tremendous flexibility in porting from
Windows<->Unix<->OS2?(God forbid)<->DOS or whatever. Merely generate the
target GNT/Object module by either recompiling or have the .INT code
converted to .GNT code on the Target machines Micro Focus compiler.

It was a brilliant concept and to this day is still brilliant.
Portability is the main virtue along with very little RTS (Run Time
Sytem) overhead making .GNT code a very viable method to emit back-end
COBOL code.

Mr. Abstract Machine
```

```
I agree with Bill on this

I have no "real" experience with AcuCOBOL or RMCOBOL. However I do have with
Microfocus, VisualAGE and Fujitsu

MicroFOCUS
I used MF cobol since the days of v3.28 for mainframe devt in many sites. I
must admit it had many problems then, but by v4+ it was really good. With
the release of MainframeExpress, the package was a dream for mainframe COBOL
devt

I did some evaluations of it a couple of years back in some of the sites I
worked, and in all cases I recommended it. Some bought the package others
didn't siting cost as the ONLY reason of rejection

I must admit though, the full package has EVERYTHING required for m/f devt,
including JCL,assembler,CICS etc, which means M/F shops that will be using
it should see (in my belief) SIGNIFICANT production increases, as well as
less stress on CPU usage, since testing execution could run outside the
mainframe.

VisualAge COBOL:
I also used this package a few years ago (up to v2.2) for another
site,setting up a mainframe DB2/COBOL devt environment on it.
The package comes in many forms, however fundamentally it comes...
1) as a stand alone devt environment, where there is NO JCL support, NO ASM
support, and the workbench is very OS2 oriented, and
2) it also comes in a configuration where it could be remotely accessing the
mainframe,so basically devt of all source occurs on the PC, but when you
execute anything it runs on the m/f.

I must admit the install set up of it also lacked a lot. It seemed to have
been very "fiddly", and it had many issues with "commits" if I remember
correctly

Because of the cost of the "remote" option, the site opted for the stand
alone setup, which after three years it abandoned.
The truth is that it is NOT as good as microfocus.

FUJITSU
This compiler is my personal choice. Great speed, very easy to use,
intuitive, and the least expensive of the above (even for the Enterprise
option)

Conclusion...
I seriously believe that the best compilers are M/F and Fujitsu. However I
feel that they target different environments
For mainframe development , and I am not talking of "some of the mainframe
devt", but rather "full m/f development", you cannot beat Microfocus

For a cheap compiler, with great environment, fast execution (on windows
platform anyway), I beleive you can't beat Fujitsu

I also feel that both RMCOBOL and AcuCOBOL would be best compared against
Fujitsu since I believe they too target the same environment.

Again this is my personal comments

Rgds
TC
```

#### ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** Abstract Machin <member32731@dbforums.com>
- **Date:** 2003-07-13T02:36:08+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3104467.1058063768@dbforums.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com>`

```

Be very careful when porting mainframe COBOL applications from MVS/VSE
to Fujitsu. Unlike Micro Focus, Fujitsu has zero support of the EBCDIC
collating sequence. The implication of this is plain old ugly. Consider
the following:

IF A > B
Where A is a PIC X(03) data field having "2B3" as its contents. Presume
B has a Value of "A26" as its content

The MVS/VSE/OS390 execution would evaluate the expression as true.
The contents of data field "A" are indeed greater than the contents of
data field "B".

If an MVS/VSE "ported" Fujitsu application executes this statement it
evaluates as false presuming the data contents are one in the same - but
represented in ASCII.

The ASCII HEX representation of data field "A" is X'324233'
The ASCII HEX representation of data field "B" is x'413236'

This versus the EBCDIC representation:

The EBCDIC value of data field "A" is X'F2C2F3'
The EBCDIC value of data field "B" is x'C1F2F6'

If one ports the COBOL application from the MVS environment, converts
the data files to ASCII, the ported Fujitsu program will not execute in
the same manner as it does on the MVS/VSE mainframe. Errors have been
introduced in the ported application when compared to the MVS execution
behavior as a function of the code points/sets. "F" > "1" in ASCII. "F"
is < "1" in EBCDIC.

What's even worse is that any comparative expression of less than,
greater than, greater than or equal, less than or equal, and the
negation of the aforementioned will FAIL if interacting with Usage
Display data fields whether they be elementary or group level (all group
level are evaluated as character data) depending upon the data presented
to the expression.

But the absolute killer here is that finding every comparative
expression and ascertaining as to whether it is interacting with
ALPHAMERIC/ALPHANUMERIC data is a trial and error process - It is data
dependent. One test may by happenstance match MVS results. Given
different input data, other results may not match MVS results.

Also consider the scanner, parsing, and lexical analysis required to
even find the expressions in a COBOL program - it isn't as easy as you
would think.

Hey Bill Klein consider this insidious example.

88    VALID-ACCOUNT  VALUE 'AAA' THRU '333'

If VALID-ACCOUNT perform foo

Generates internal code that states the evaluated data must be
NOT LESS  than 'AAA' and NOT GREATER than '333'. Lack of an EBCDIC
collating sequence  or at a minimum the lack of a directive to
internally convert the ASCII data to EBCDIC and then do the compare in
Fujitsu just butchers the execution of the if VALID-ACCOUNT statement.

When I see people talk about the speed of Fujitsu Compile/Execution I
cringe because testing a ported application takes forever under Fujitsu.

You can value the quick compile and executon speeds but I don't
think a developer can ever recover the tremendous expense of testing
MVS COBOL applications ported to a WINDOWS platform with Fujitsu -
it is incredibly time consuming , error prone, and data dependent
upon test suites.

I have several other examples of incompatabilities between Fujitsu
execution and MVS execution - but the above example(s) is by far
the worst.

Teaser: when is -1 considered -4 (SIGNED USAGE DISPLAY) ?

Mr. Abstract Machine
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>

- **Message-ID:** `<besl9c$p5p$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
Fujitsu NetCOBOL for Windows V7 definitely *DOES* include support for EBCDIC
Collating Sequence. (I checked the documentation) what I could NOT find was
whether or not it has a "full" EBCDIC "run-time environment" (things like
DISPLAY of a data item with EBCDIC data in it).

This may or may not (I think NOT - but am uncertain of this) exist in some
Fujitsu product.  Possibly some other user can answer this.

This distinction is IMPORTANT for those using the PC for "development" of
applications intended to run on the mainframe for production.  It is less
important for those doing a "one-time" portation to the PC (or Unix) for
production work.  You do need to know what does and does not require
changing for these, but it is a "one-off" task.

P.S.  Of even more interest to me is the fact that the Fujitsu NetCOBOL for
.NET product (I think both V1 and V2) includes no support for ASCII *files*.
The documentation indicates that only Unicode "files" are supported - but
that ASCII support *may* be added in the future.
```

#### ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** Abstract Machin <member32731@dbforums.com>
- **Date:** 2003-07-13T04:10:04+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3104509.1058069404@dbforums.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com>`

```

Mr Klein, please correct me if I am off base. It seems to me that Micro
Focus has a full EBCDIC collating support feature invoked via the
compiler directive CHARSET(EBCDIC) - which interacts with the EBCDIC
based data. I think Micro Focus also contemplated execution in an ASCII
environment (i.e. with ASCII data) but wished to preserve the EBCDIC
collating sequence on certain comparative expressions. I believe that
support was implemented by the NATIVE(EBCDIC) compiler directive.

In my previous example using Fujitsu in an ASCII world (where the MVS
data has been translated to the equivalent ASCII codeset) use of the
Micro Focus Compiler and the Native(EBCDIC) directive would facilitate
proper execution with ASCII based MVS born data.

I wonder if that intermediate/internal translation to EBCDIC was
implemented on START MY-FILE MY-KEY > 'A' - I can't remember.

I guess my point is why in the world would anyone consider Fujitsu to
port MVS applications and data to a Windows Platform? Micro Focus is in
my estimation the only easy and reliably implemented solution.

Also consider the implications of SIGN(ASCII) versus SIGN(EBCDIC) in an
ASCII world interacting with translated EBCDIC data. Micro Focus wins
these kind of account issues hands down with these cross platform
collating sequence issues enabled via compiler directives. I don't think
Fujitsu has any compiler directives/option to address any collating
sequence issues.

I know there are still problems with codeset collating. For instance MS
SQL Server uses by default a folded ASCII collating sequence. This
unfortunately puts special characters like a "$" (I think x'2d') ahead
of a space (a hex '20'). The implication is that a typical COBOL
match/merge program using default MS SQL collating sequence and
VSAM/QSAM data may not collate correctly (even if using Micro Focus).

Furthermore, the Windows based version of SyncSort also has a
non-standard collating sequence. So if a program step sorts a data set,
passes the sorted data set onto a SQL and QSAM match merge program it is
possible to have 3 disparate codesets/codepoints all of which WERE
presumed to be identical.

I would guess the answer to "why use Fujitsu" is the purported fabled
speed of Fujitsu. I'll trade speed for integrity every time.

Mr. Abstract Machine
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** "Gary" <IDontThink@so.com.so>
- **Date:** 2003-07-13T16:41:51+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<jZfQa.47720$OZ2.7433@rwcrnsc54>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3104509.1058069404@dbforums.com>`

```
"Abstract Machin" <member32731@dbforums.com> wrote:
> I guess my point is why in the world would anyone consider Fujitsu to
> port MVS applications and data to a Windows Platform? Micro Focus is in
> my estimation the only easy and reliably implemented solution.

Many enterprises are porting/migrating MVS applications to Windows and other
(lower-cost) platforms; notably Linux on the same MVS hardware. But support
for ASCII and/or EBCDIC really isn't the point; there are many (complex)
aspects to moving MVS applications to "open" (or "distributed") systems.

And, although support for EBCDIC can be useful, IMO, if you're moving to an
ASCII environment, you will want (now or later) your application to be ASCII
also -- that way, you can continue to evolve/extend your application with
other distributed technologies. Both Micro Focus (with Net Express and
Server Express) and Fujitsu provide development environments for exploiting
your COBOL applications in these environments.

If, on the other hand, you're simply looking at "rehosting" an MVS
application under Micro Focus Mainframe Express, then yes, it's a pretty
powerful replication of the mainframe environment on a PC.

Gary.
```

```
[You missed the point entirely. There are at least 3 options to move an
MVS COBOL application to a Windows platfrom.

1) Rewrite the application in some "C" C#, Java, C++ language
2) Purchase an off the shelf package that more or less mimes the
   original COBOL application/system
3) Port the existing COBOL code to a new platform.

Point 1 has a set of signifcant problems - Packed Decimal Data fields,
IMS, CICS, DFSORT, MVS Utilities, assembler subroutines/mains, PL/I,
Fortran, Easytrieve, report writers, UCC 2-11, JCL, JES, IDCAMS, VSAM,
BDAM, DMS, SMS, SMF, RACF, ACF2, spanned data sets - None of which are
reflected in the "New Age" programming and systems methodologies BUT are
of SIGNIFICANT use to MVS data centers.

Point 2 is fraught with millions of bucks of conversion
adaptation expense
I jhave yet to see it done correctly

Point 3 is viable if done correctly.

There is an implication in your response that more or less discards
giga-bytes (if not tera-bytes)  of existing MVS data sets. I think in
your scenario you advocate starting the whole IT operation from scratch
- ignoring the legacy data.
NOT!

Point 3 is interesting. It more or less requires putting a new face on
legacy COBOL code (business rules). Whether it be .NET or Oracle's/Sun's
look and feel, or whatever. My point is that if any given company is
going to preserve the MVS functionality they should approach it wisely.
I would suspect the MicroSoft is out of its league when contemplating
porting MVS application to a Windows environment.

My suspicion is that most MVS accounts will remain hardcore MVS
accounts. Moving to a tinker toy like OS, like Windows or Linux is a
horror story of security breakdowns that are not tolerated in the MVS
world. RACF/ACF2 have absolute integrity. You cannot even position a
read head on an MVS DASD device without proper and strict authorization
from RACF/ACF2. The focus of supposed security in Windows is infant when
compared to security on MVS.

Don't get me entirely wrong I like windows for its word processing, the
internet, E-Mail, games (solitare) but beyond that I wouldn't trust
Windows to even balance my checkbook.

Mr. Abstract Machine
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-07-16T16:34:14+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3f157d22.180906992@news.optonline.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3115138.1058322227@dbforums.com> <217e491a.0307152202.74a2a170@posting.google.com>`

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>I have more radios than TVs, in what way have TVs _replaced_ anything?

I once wrote:

 " Revolutions do not succeed just because they are technically feasible.
Failure of the old system is also required.  For example, television was
technically feasible in the 1920's but didn't replace movies until the 50's.
Why?  Because movies still "worked". Air travel was expensive until railroads
became senile in the mid 60's."

Other examples: Cable began replacing broadcast TV when networks became
complacent. Paid labor replaced slaves in the US agricultural South only when
slavery stopped working (became illegal).

Mainframes today do pretty much what they always did. At what point did they
"stop working?" Not during the MSDOS '80s era, when PCs and Unixen offered
similar features, the only advantage being lower price. PCs pulled ahead in the
mid '90s by offering GUI, new programming paradigms that provided a real
advantage (for a change), a variety of hardware unavailable on a mainframe, a
WIDE area network (internet) and liberation from the tyranny of the IT
department. 

Just as the movie industry offers its wares on DVD, mainframe manufacturers play
me-too catch-up by offering server functionality, Java and Linux. Playing by the
enemy's rules was a tacit admission they'd lost industry  leadership. Does that
mean mainframes stopped working? It does in the minds of managers who want to be
'progressive' -- far enough on the leading edge to have an advantage over
competition. Those who are indifferent can easily be nudged over by  success
stories in airline magazines or pressure from recent MBA graduates. Few managers
want to say 'We may be technically archaic but we have SMS and CA utilities.
Nyah, nyah.'

Mainframes have stopped working when functionality is defined dynamically rather
than statically. PCs offer time-to-market today and a growth path tomorrow, both
of which are a competitive advantage.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Sabre conversion was Re: Cobol development environment

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Clark F. Morris, Jr." <cfmtech@istar.ca>
- **Date:** 2003-07-18T09:33:51-03:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3F17E92F.CD62C533@istar.ca>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3115138.1058322227@dbforums.com> <217e491a.0307152202.74a2a170@posting.google.com> <3f157d22.180906992@news.optonline.com> <3119244.1058391633@dbforums.com> <3f1600cc.214618567@news.optonline.com> <bf69ls$81k$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
They are trying to move from Transaction Processing Facility (TPF)
formerly Airline Control Program to either HP-UX or Tandem.  TPF
originally was supervisor mode only, is very fast and was designed to
handle thousands of transactions per minute on hardware outclassed by
todays Celerons.  TPF originally was assembler only but I understand it
now supports C.  COBOL was never supported in that environment.  It
still may be an environment where the transactions are effectively part
of the operating system.

Howard Brazee wrote:
> 
> On 17-Jul-2003, robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote:
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Are they moving from mainframes to PCs?   Or is this a red herring?
```

```
into it. It's my understanding that they are largely using C++.

entirely in IBM assembler.
```

```
"Donald Tees" <Donald_Tees@sympatico.ca> wrote

> Well Howard, I do not know of any computers anywhere that run anything with
> no data ever being stored on them. You, in fact, are also saying that PD's
> are "incapable" of handling important data. I will stand by my statement ...
> I think both both of you wrong.

(I assume PD == PC) 

One problem is that the term 'PC' may not be the same to everyone. PC
stands for 'Personal Computer' - ie for one person (at a time). 
Typically a Pentium class machine running Windows 98 or XP.

Some seem to think that everything that is not a mainframe is thus a
'PC'.  Or that anything with an Intel (or AMD) processor (or several)
is a 'PC'.

If a machine is used as a server - whether that be with Unix, Linux,
Windows, BSD, QNX, AIX, or even MacOS, then it is not a 'PC' even
though it may have some residual architectural derivation from the
original IBM PC design.

In fact few seem to understand the derivation of 'mainframe computer'
and why the term should no longer apply. Many electrical and
electronic installations (power stations, phone exchanges, etc) are
assembled into 'frames' or structural frameworks which may be covered
to make cabinets.  The central one is the 'main frame', the others
being peripheral or secondary.  The 'mainframe computer' is one where
several cabinets are used to house the whole system and the central
one is the CPU.

This structure no longer applies - 'mainframes' amy be small cabinets,
even PC sized.
 
> AND *I* never said mainframes are obsolete. I said they are capable of full
> fledged IT functions, like it or not. Not every IT function, but certainly
> lots and lots of it.  

Exactly. And PC

> Just about every PC on the market today can both
> out-perform and is *far* more reliable than any mainframe I worked on 30
> years back.

When I worked on mainframes 30 years ago they would stay up for weeks
or months between scheduled maintenance.  Most PCs existing today (ie
most Windows machines) require rebooting at intervals otherwise they
will become unstable.

Many 'PC's running as servers do, in fact, stay up for months.  I've
seen Novell servers that have been up for 3 years - but then they can
upgrade the OS 'in flight' too.  Unix and Linux machines being up for
a year or two are not uncommon.

So again, what is a 'PC' ?

I wouldn't run any critical data on a Windows 98 machine, or on a
machine with a single IDE drive regardless of OS.

Build a machine with a server OS on multi-CPUs with RAID drives and it
is _not_ a PC.

> In fact, with mainframes adopting UNIX, and PC's adopting Linux, I'd say the
> lines are getting blurrier and blurrier.  If anything is going to make Cobol
> more viable, that evolution certainly will.

Computers that had the same CPUs as 'PC's have been used for Unix and
other Multi-user OSes for 25 years.  MP/M, Oasys and BOS date from
1978 or so.  Xenix on 8086 from 1980.  I was doing Cobol on those.
```

```
Me too, and that is the point.  The arguement has been going on since about
1982, and it is the same arguement.  Then I heard it about the 360 and the
altos. Or the PDP-11 and the Vax.  I mean, it is getting tiring.

With the convirgence of Unix scale OS's on *both* sets of machines, the
lines are blurrier and blurier still. I can see a strong argument for a
common OS right from mainframe to desktop workstation, and such a beast
could be running the same high level language everywhere.  Cobol is the only
language that I could do that with, as far as I know.  It was true 20 years
ago, and it is true today.

/topic shift/ Even for fail-safe hardware, though, you can use PC's.  I was
looking at a $150 CPU board with a raid controler built in the other day.
two of those with 8 disks(four/machine) mirroring  each other would make a
pretty safe personal storage device.  Stick a DVD writer on both, dedicate
them to that function and never upgrade them until you replace them, and it
would be bank quality, albeit very small scale. You could be talking a
terrabyte for about two grand.

Donald
```

```
I've been told that Unisys sold and installed more ClearPath HMP NX/LX
series systems (the direct descendants of the Burroughs Large System) in the
fourth quarter of 2002 than in any quarter in the more-than-four-decade
history of this system architecture.

I originally missed, and based on where this thread has gone I suspect
others have missed, an important point in the quotation "IBM sold 70 new
mainframe customers ...".

That's not 70 new mainframe *sales*, that's 70 *customers new to IBM,
purchasing mainframes*  who didn't previously have IBM equipment in that
role (or didn't have equipment in that role at all).  That actually does
strike me as a statistic that's not entirely implausible.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
zOS, if you want it.  I know a company that is considering just that
to replace their MP3000 and OS/390 release 2.9.
```

```
Robert Wagner wrote:
> 
> Arnold Trembley <arnold.trembley@worldnet.att.net> wrote:
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> The number of concurrent TSO/E sessions is limited to eight in z/OS.e"

Yes, that's all true...

> 
> http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/E0Z2B132/1.10?DT=20030428160747

BUT...there is an interesting quote on the web page above:

"The only IBM server on which z/OS.e runs is the IBM e(logo)server
zSeries 800 (z800) server. (z/OS
runs on any IBM e(logo)server zSeries server.) z/OS.e requires the
z800 to run in LPAR mode (not basic mode). (z/OS can run in either
mode.)"

Note that IBM says "z/OS runs on any IBM zSeries server", which means
that z/OS will run on a Z990 T-Rex, a z900, or a z800 (which will be
the smallest new IBM mainframe you can buy).
 
> 
> >And IBM will be perfectly happy to sell you a z800 with the full-price
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> which is why they created z/OS.e.

The whole point of z/OS.e is for IBM to sell entry-level z-boxes to
e-business shops.  That market is probably not interested in COBOL,
CICS, IMS, Fortran, or PL/I.  They want to run web servers, which is
why IBM created z/OS.e.  

But IBM also has a problem with "small mainframes".  Computer
Associates and Compuware are killing the market for entry-level
mainframes.  If you're a small shop, you can afford to upgrade an
MP2000 or MP3000 to a z800 or a small Z900.  The hardware cost is
reasonable, often cheaper.  But Computer Associates and Compuware will
raise your license fees by MILLIONS per year, because they bill based
on the MIPS rating of your mainframe, and newer "small" mainframes
have much higher MIPS ratings.  The small mainframe shops want to
upgrade to supported hardware/Operating System, but they can't afford
the third-party software license fees.  They're being priced out of
the mainframe market.

Even for a large mainframe shop, upgrading to a bigger, faster,
cheaper mainframe often means increasing the total DP budget, just to
cover third party software licenses.  

See Bill Klein's posting on "IBM 'Mainframes' on the PC", where he
mentions z900 emulation on Intel boxes using Flex-ES and Hercules.
```

```
| example).

You can't use MIPS to compare processors with different instruction sets.

The mainframe processes strings, the Intel processes characters.
What do you think a null delimited character strings is for?
(loop 'til you find a null.)
Try using packed decimal on the Intel, and you'll find you have to program a
loop to do it.

If you want to compare processors, run a benchmark.
```

```
Guys, if it's not being picked up properly, I am infusing this with a
great deal of sarcasim, but still trying to make the point. I actually
know who Mr. Abstract Machine is, but I'm not going to divulge his or
her identity. The real point I'm trying make is that people sometimes
attack what they don't understand and in terms of COBOL in particular, I
believe that the only chance for COBOL's future is to get it off the
mainframe in most instances and onto PC platforms where software and
tools are much richer and far more modern. The mainframe has become  and
anchor tied around COBOL's neck, and the refusal by many to fully
embrace the new PC platforms with their COBOL applications simply spells
doom for them in the future. If they are not migrated, then they will
simply be replaced by other technologies over time. And so I must admit
I get more than a bit agitated when I seem someone posting a note
spelling out all of these purported "warnings" for not migrating when I
know good and well it is quite doable, have done so myself, and continue
to help others so such. I don't subscribe to the chicken little "the sky
is falling - the data ain't EBCDIC" way of thinking..... And COBOL will
continue to die a slow death as long as people hold on to this
entrenched way of thinking....
Thanks,
-Howard
```

```
I don't have an emotional need to have COBOL survive.   I want my job to
survive.   Where I have worked, COBOL has been on the mainframe, if we want to
work on PCs, we switch tools.   The hard part is to get companies to pay
attention to their promises to give their valuable COBOL programmers experience
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com>
- **Date:** 2003-07-16T20:29:12+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3118793.1058387352@dbforums.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3118241.1058378028@dbforums.com> <bf46lr$8ul$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```

Yes, this is certainly a travesty that often exists in large companies
with COBOL shops. One of the greatest pleasures in my job is training
mainframe COBOL programmers how to use PC COBOL tools, such as the
Fujitsu.NET compiler. The reactions are sometimes just overwhelming -
like slaves being freed or someone coming out of a dark prison cell into
the sunlight. And the real shame  is that these programmers are ones who
know and understand the company's business and are far superior than
recent college grad VB and Java programmers at designing and
implementing systems. Ask and Java or VB programmer what a "two phase
commit" is and watch the response. Mainframers think in transactional
commit/rollback ways that most PC programmers have little concept of.
And it's a trememdous shame that this experience and talent isn't
leverage more often on the PC side of the house. One think I've been
saying for nearly 20 years and it still holds water "I would rather take
a mainframe COBOL programmer and teach he or she how to work on the PC
than take a PC programmer and try to teach he/he to work on the
mainframe"...
Thanks,
-Howard

with the new tools.
```

```
Have you ever heard of INTERTEST? And it is just one of many. I used one
called ADS and Cole Software markets another powerful one whose name
escapes me at the moment. On VM systems there is one called PER from
University of Waterloo. These debuggers have been available on mainframes
for many, many years and they are being used.

There are as many vastly different lifetimes of experience and views here
as there are posters. No one of us knows it all and we most certainly don't
know what the future holds. It beats me why we are so quick to generalize
so widely based on our own relatively meager experiences.
```

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-07-18T20:10:34+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3f17ac1d_4@news.athenanews.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3118241.1058378028@dbforums.com> <bf46lr$8ul$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3118793.1058387352@dbforums.com> <bf4nfo$3qt$1@panix1.panix.com> <3119847.1058404001@dbforums.com> <01c34c06$b774b660$118bf243@chottel> <3f1683eb_5@news.athenanews.com> <01c34cda$5a240f40$3ec2f943@chottel>`

```
If everybody in the Fortress was as motivated as you are Charles, there
would be a lot less gnashing of teeth.

I admire the fact that you are diligently pursuing the acquisition of new
skills and I really hope it pays off for you.

I take your point on the Java/Websphere approach; for someone in a mainframe
environment this might indeed be a very sensible way to go.

.NET is showing great promise and it has the backing of MS, but it is a bit
of an unknown quantity. The jury is still out as to whether it will be the
answer so many are hoping for, and whether it will be readily embraced by
the Client/Server community. The principles are great; level playing field,
source language independence, all good stuff, but it seems to be taking
longer than was expected to gain universal acceptance.

The bottom line is that that it is not mutaully exclusive and one does not
preclude the other. It is useful to have Java AND .NET.

You say Java doesn't "thrill" you...could I recommend a book? It is "Java 2
in 24 hours" by Rogers Cadenhead. This is the best book on Java I've come
across for the purpose of learning the language and being able to use it.
(There are better books for pure Java reference once you HAVE learned it...)
The style is an absolute delight and it is written with a citric sense of
humour that has you laughing while you learn. I believe that anyone who
learns Java from this book will be encouraged to keep going just for the
jokes... I found it superb and learned most of my basic Java from it. (I
still treasure the Java Crap game I wrote (with help from Cadenhead) in hour
22 of this book.)

"All the jobs going overseas..." Let's hope not. If they do, it is only a
matter of time before the rates go up. (Once the Sub-Continent realises it
has the "technology monopoly" they will hike the rates. As Indian
programmers become more affluent, they will expect and demand more.
Eventually, they will become the victims of their own success, but that will
be far too late for many Western Programmers...

Pete.

<snip>
```

```
Not sure, Howard. I would expect that to be the case, but I don't live in
the US and I don't know whether you are being ironic or mean this literally.

Many factors affect the cost of manufactured goods (tariffs, subsidies,
inflation, etc.).

Certainly where I live the cost of manufactured goods rises every year (even
when they are made here). I bought my Mother a washing machine 8 years ago
and remember paying around $300 for it. Today the same (or technically
equivalent) machine is just under $1000. I haven't done the sums on
inflation in NZ but I wouldn't mind betting that the average wage has not
tripled in this time...

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

_(reply depth: 12)_

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2003-07-21T15:03:32+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bfgvc4$8tc$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3118241.1058378028@dbforums.com> <bf46lr$8ul$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3118793.1058387352@dbforums.com> <bf4nfo$3qt$1@panix1.panix.com> <3119847.1058404001@dbforums.com> <01c34c06$b774b660$118bf243@chottel> <3f1683eb_5@news.athenanews.com> <01c34cda$5a240f40$3ec2f943@chottel> <3f17ac1d_4@news.athenanews.com> <bf90l0$fvv$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3f18a1d2_7@news.athenanews.com>`

```

On 18-Jul-2003, "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> > Just like the cost of manufactured goods went up when Japanese labor got
> > more expensive?
>
> Not sure, Howard. I would expect that to be the case, but I don't live in
> the US and I don't know whether you are being ironic or mean this literally.

I was being ironic.   When Japanese labor got more expensive, they did what the
U.S. did and switched to technologies that required higher skills, while other
countries took over the lower end manufacturing.


> Certainly where I live the cost of manufactured goods rises every year (even
> when they are made here). I bought my Mother a washing machine 8 years ago
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> tripled in this time...

Washers haven't increased that rapidly in Colorado.

But the test is that we have more stuff now.  My kids have more stuff than I had
at their age (and they have the same size families that I did).   This means
stuff is cheaper.

With software, we have a couple of different pressures.   When one country gets
more expensive labor, the easier programming will go elsewhere.   As software
components become more of commodities, components will be designed wherever they
can be created the most cost effectively.   People who have the ability to do
the work, but who need the money the most will underbid others.

This will mean that us programmers in rich countries who charge a lot will need
to change our focus away from the coding and more towards the business needs of
our employers.
```

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:bfgvc4$8tc$1@peabody.colorado.edu...
>
> On 18-Jul-2003, "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:
<snipped interesting description>
>
> This will mean that us programmers in rich countries who charge a lot will
need
> to change our focus away from the coding and more towards the business
needs of
> our employers.

The above encapsulates something I have been gently trying to point out for
some years in this forum.

If it doesn't happen, the Business will start "doing it for themselves".
There are already intimations of this. The last contract I did it was very
apparent that the Business are no longer totally dependent on IT to produce
IT solutions.

While we are fiercely contesting the finer points of COBOL code, the rest of
the world are finding other ways to get solutions. I also believe that the
Waterfall has been a cause of poor delivery to the Business and this has
caused them to seek alternatives, (if they had been happy with the service
they received from IT, there would be no need to look for other solutions).
However, that is entirely a personal opinion and I accept it is arguable.

Pete.
```

```
My conclusion is that there are interests which perceive a maintenance of 
the status quo ante to be superior to a use of these tools... what's 
yours?

>

discouraged.  Given that (as long as resources used to not have an adverse 
impact on the Production Proocesses) Just About *Any* Debugger Is Better

>    for any language on any platform....  So the sale of Workbench was
>    quite easy after people recognized the value and cost benefit of
>    getting their programmers off the mainframe....

You'll find little argument from me about MicroFocus; I began using it in 
1983 and still have a copy of version 1.7something that fits on two 720K 
floppies.  What remains, however, is what I mentioned earlier... that the 
'greater shame' is that there are tools that exist and, in some shops, 
already in place... and their use is neglected or discouraged.

This is, in my opinion, not a fault of the technology but of the 
Management of the personnel and resources involved.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Harley" <dennis.harleyNoSpam@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 2003-07-17T11:54:54+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<i8wRa.59154$3o3.3936080@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3118241.1058378028@dbforums.com> <bf46lr$8ul$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3118793.1058387352@dbforums.com> <bf4nfo$3qt$1@panix1.panix.com>`

```

<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:bf4nfo$3qt$1@panix1.panix.com...
| In article <3118793.1058387352@dbforums.com>,
| hhinman  <member30421@dbforums.com> wrote:
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
| long to figure out and just slows everyone down.'

People do tend to use Intertest, in CICS.
The problem with the batch animators is that you have to allocate the files
under ISPF, which is above the capability of many programmers.
One programmer wrote a REXX procedure to read the JCL, and create the
ALLOCATE commands, few of us even knew about it.

Personally, I use animators very very infrequently.
I can find out what I need to know using the text editor.

If someone uncovers a "bug" in one of my programs using an animator, my
response is recreate the problem without the animator. (I got burned bad,
wasting half a day tracking down a "bug" only to find the person was
modifying Working Storage, and caused the problem himself.)

Testing requires running a full set of test cases, sometimes numbering in
the hundreds, or thousands.
Trying to test using an animator is next to impossible.
Even if you have only 15 test cases, if you find a problem at test case 10,
and correct the code, you have to start over at test case 1.
```

```
finding a 'bug' in code after any sort of modification.
```

```
claim they did.
```

```
I usually ask people to review my code.
As a consultant, I use the excuse that I won't be around to maintain it, and
I want to make sure they understand it.

Sometimes, the passive method works.

On one project I was working with a group at another site.
Their copy books all had hard coded high level qualifiers.
I put a copy book in the source library with modifiable high level
qualifiers, and told them the copy book was in the library.
When I looked at their copy books 2 days later, they all had the modifiable
high level qualifiers, with a message that said "So you can use the copy
book more than once in the same program".
```

```
Howard Brazee wrote:

> On 18-Jul-2003, LX-i <LXi0007@Netscape.net> wrote:
> 
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> having an in-line perform?

Well, as I told someone else, that was an omission.  There are a host of 
things that I don't agree with in that example above...

1.  Perform, Varying, and Until should start the three lines of the 
perform.  It's a style issue, but it's one of our style standards (that 
even predates me).

2.  Perform Thru - again, it's a style issue, and also a standard that 
was there when I arrived.  I've seen many flow problem arise from use of 
Perform Thru.

3.  Yes, an inline perform would be much more appropriate, IMO.

I'm not going to gripe about all uppercase - there's no standard, and 
I'm not going to quibble over case with someone who's been coding for 
longer than I've been alive.  :)  The above issues arise from specific 
problems I've encountered during the time I've been coding.
```

```
If I may throw in a few comments.  This looks fine when together, but if 
the 234-paras were several hunderd or thousand lines away from the 
perform it takes on a new light.  I often see crap that does absolutely 
nothing except for one iteration becase something has been added to the 
234-para without recognizing that it is in a loop.  Or even worse, 
occasionally you will see the para altering a variable used in the until 
clause (WS-COUNT in this case) in a strange way.

Simplicity is a good thing.  If it can be written in 4 lines instead of 
9, then the 4 line version is at least half as easy to grasp.
```

- **Message-ID:** `<vhqi8ubkhjai11@corp.supernews.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<joe_zitzelberger-59FAB8.08124122072003@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3118793.1058387352@dbforums.com> <bf4nfo$3qt$1@panix1.panix.com> <i8wRa.59154$3o3.3936080@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <bf64tm$ceh$1@panix1.panix.com> <%1GRa.59991$3o3.3978142@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <vhgvqnp2g8fmd2@corp.supernews.com> <bfgvhr$8to$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <vhp0orit2vq3be@corp.supernews.com> <joe_zitzelberger-59FAB8.08124122072003@corp.supernews.com>`

```
Joe Zitzelberger wrote:
> If I may throw in a few comments.  This looks fine when together, but if 
> the 234-paras were several hunderd or thousand lines away from the 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> clause (WS-COUNT in this case) in a strange way.

:)  This is more common that you'd think in our code.  Here's an example 
(and I'll try to make sure it's syntactically correct...)

      PERFORM 123-LOOK-AT-ME THRU 123-EXIT.

[500 lines later]

  123-LOOK-AT-ME.
      ADD 1 TO LOOP-COUNT.
      IF LOOP-COUNT > 500
        GO 123-EXIT.
      [more stuff]
      GO 123-LOOK-AT-ME.
  123-EXIT.
      EXIT.

Now, to the compiler, there's nothing wrong with this code.  And, it 
executes properly and does what it needs to do.  However, in my opinion, 
  it is much more meaningful to use Perform Varying in this case.  It 
has the additional benefit of another part of the code being able to 
only do iterations between 100 and 200, for example.

Having said that, I am guilty of intentionally coding a Perform Thru in 
the last month.  It was one paragraph of "edits" - basically, it was 
trying to find a reason not to continue.  When it found an errant 
condition, it would set a reject code and go to the exit.  In the code 
that performed it, the next statement checked the reject code, and if it 
was > 0, it would terminate processing.  That's really the only case 
that I'll ever use Perform Thru...  :)

> Simplicity is a good thing.  If it can be written in 4 lines instead of 
> 9, then the 4 line version is at least half as easy to grasp.

Did you mean to say "half as easy", or "twice as easy"?  :)
```

```
>Robert Wagner wrote:

Same here - one error you're out. With only 71 positions to give an error
message it's rather hard to show more than one error at a time (yes - there are
80 positions on a line - 1 for the attribute and 7 for the message number {shop
standard} leaves 71 for me).  The message 'Fix your blankity blank errors' 
wouldn't go over too well.  At one shop I worked at they did edit the whole
screen and put out a message 'You have X errors' where X was the number of
errors on the screen.  All were highlighted.  It was in production about 3 days
when the team leader got a call 'It's nice that your system edits the whole
screen and I know I have 5 errors - BUT WHAT THE HECK ARE THEY?'  Back to one
at a time.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

_(reply depth: 16)_

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca>
- **Date:** 2003-07-25T04:47:01+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3F20A93A.502BDF1B@shaw.ca>`
- **References:** `<vhqqpmeposo77b@corp.supernews.com> <20030724224956.05032.00000446@mb-m26.aol.com>`

```


YukonMama wrote:

> >Robert Wagner wrote:
> >
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> at a time.

There just can't be a hard set of rules on validation errors - it depends upon the
environment, although I do see what Robert is suggesting.

Once buzzing around the Web I had one of those pages offering something, but you
had to sign up - the usual first name, last name, address Line 1, address Line 2
etc.
I didn't want to sign up but I accidentally hit the <Submit> button - result, a
messagebox :-

        <First name can't be blank
        <Last Name can't be blank
        <Address Line 1 can't be blank
        <City can't be blank
        <Zipcode can't be blank etc. etc....

        <OK>        <Cancel>

I call that a 'Mickey Mouse' Validation - it's clean cut and can be validated in
one swoop after the <Submit>. The Mickey Mouse approach is fine where it is
relevant.

However like Daniel, and I'm talking inter-active with a Dialog, I have one screen
with 24 pressure stress values (pic 9.9). The rules that apply - each ascending
value must be greater than the previous, although intermediary columns can be
zeroes -

    1.2        0.0            1.4

So if the third read '1.1' - then we have an error. No point attempting to validate
the remaining columns - they also will probably be out of whack. Tell the user
Column 3 is invalid - and HOPE they look at the screen, to correct the remainder if
necessary.

Eileen, with due respect ma'am - I think your one error approach is a bit crappy
<G>. Let's go back to Noah's time, first system I designed which went live in '66.
Order/Delivery Note for dairy products which was also a key-punch (paper tape)
document.

    ** Account #
    ** Salesman's # (this was before Women's Lib <G>)

    From memory about 15 lines for Products :-
    ** Product Code
    Quantity
    ++ Price - shillings and pence only (e.g. 323/10 per cwt.)

*** These had a Mod 11 check digit (approx. 99% plus accuracy). If the Mod 11 check
failed, Customer File or Product File Lookup would probably reject the document in
the next phase of the system.

+++ Prices - shillings and pence were added up (by the depot clerical staff) to
give a hash total - hopefully to alleviate as many pricing errors, (mis-punching)
as possible. In practice it worked very well.

No screens around then so reports of batch acceptance were going to the good ole
line-printer barrel on 11 x 14. Within the Computer Centre we had our own control
clerks, (2)  who corrected errors. Of course the document contained lots of
hand-written stuff to identify the above 'keyed' areas.

Any check digit failure or hash total failure - generated an error line. If it was
as a result of an hash total failure then all product lines were printed. Each
error printed an appropriate message. Of course the computer 'bit' tied in with the
whole system :-

Account #'s - Thanks to my buddy the O&M Manager who was an ex-RAF squadron leader,
(and as DD would write, he is long gone and with the angels), the depots were
supplied with Addressograph plates (anybody remember them ?). So the distribution
depot staff imprinted the Account Number and Customer Name/Address on to the
delivery note - then they transcribed the Account # into the key-punch area.

Salesmen's' #'s - our control staff had a list of these by Region.

Product #'s - They had our Catalogue of Products.

To have rejected a document solely because the Account # or Salesman's # was
invalid would have been grossly inefficient. We validated all remaining lines
regardless ( or 'irregardless' if your last name is Hinmann <G>). We had a 4-day
turn around between receiving the delivery notes, validating them and printing the
invoices for mailing.

Methinks that old system, (on an ICT 1500 with 64K (??? - porous memory) ),  was
better than yours YukonMama <G>.

Nearly forgot - before somebody jumps in and asks,  "Why didn't you have your
prices in a Price/Product File ?" - we got our Sales Ledger people to do an
analysis of invoices over a two-week period. There were some 2,000 + variations
from the official prices. Folks you know salesmen, "Tell you what - if you take 50
cheddar blocks instead of just two, I'll let you have them at 323/00 per cwt.,
instead of 323/10 per cwt.". The pricing feature may well have been resolved and
computerized long after I left, but not back at that point in the Dark Ages..

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

```
Ok, how about context-sensitive error messages? You hilight all the error
fields, display the first error message and put the cursor on that field. The
user corrects it and tabs to the next error field. If (s)he cannot understand
what's wrong, hitting a PF key displays its error message.
```

```
>
>That's nice... and how many programs do you think you'd be able to modify
>(and using which job-code for time-accounting purposes) before you were
>told 'That's not the way errors are handled here, change it back'?

Depends on the shop culture, visibility of the system and urgency of the
deadline.

Uptight mainframe: high probability of rejection under customary conditions. As
deadline nears and formalism is abandoned, anything that 'gets the job done'
will pass muster. Crisis conditions are opportunities to do real programming.
Some mainframe shops are in crisis mode always; everything's a emergency. Those
are good places to work. 

Regular mainframe: it would be seen as a learning experience. These people are
starved for new ideas.

Client/server: a pat on the back.

Web: no notice, an expected improvement.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

_(reply depth: 18)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2003-07-25T15:47:20-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0307251447.da13783@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<vhqqpmeposo77b@corp.supernews.com> <20030724224956.05032.00000446@mb-m26.aol.com> <3f20f8b8.30297531@news.optonline.com> <bfr8vt$4sf$1@panix1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote 

> That's nice... and how many programs do you think you'd be able to modify
> (and using which job-code for time-accounting purposes) before you were
> told 'That's not the way errors are handled here, change it back'?

With interactive system I handle the fields one by one. The user
cannot get past the Customer code field without entering a valid one
(or getting a selection list or entering a new account or ..) and then
is listed the account details, status, comments etc, before
progressing to the next field.  There can only be one error at a time.

HTML/CGI Web based systems are a bit more difficult.  There has to be
several fields in each step to keep the interactions down due to
delays, but I have previously worked around this by having, say, an
input step for each line item in an order system.  The user can put in
just the product code (of course they can also search, select from
list etc) or can put in all required items for the line (quantity,
non-standard price, discount rate, etc).  There is also a 'complete'
check box.  The program will keep returning the line until the
'complete' box is clicked and all errors cleared.  So if the user is
confident they can put in all fields and mark 'complete' and go for
it, or they just just put in the code and see what comes up.

If its wrong (but accepted) they can click the 'delete this line' tag
and reenter or the 'amend this line' tag.

JavaScript is an option for validation, but I feel shouldn't be
required.  Make it helpful it it runs, if it isn't enabled then make
sure the system can still be used.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bfsds2$nf7$1@panix1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<vhqqpmeposo77b@corp.supernews.com> <3f20f8b8.30297531@news.optonline.com> <bfr8vt$4sf$1@panix1.panix.com> <217e491a.0307251447.da13783@posting.google.com>`

```
In article <217e491a.0307251447.da13783@posting.google.com>,
Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>docdwarf@panix.com wrote 
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>progressing to the next field.  There can only be one error at a time.

I've worked in shops where one error at a time is tossed back but all
fields are checked... in reverse order, of course, so that the last Length
field to which one moves -1 is the one to which the cursor is positioned.  
I've worked in shops where all errors (up to however many can be explained
in the ERRMSG line(s)) are highlighted... then there was one place where I
was putting together a screen and I asked the user how he wanted the
errors to be handled; 'Make 'em all obvious', he said, 'and don't bother
me for more, I'm a Big Picture Man.'

Came time to test... and he got to watch over the shoulder of the
data-entry girl (they were called 'girls' back then) as she keyed in the
date, hit Enter... and saw every field in error thrown back in hilighted,
inverse, blinking video.  Her response was a look of horror and the onset
of tears, his response was 'What kind of crap it this?', my reply was
'Well, they sure are obvious, aren't they?'

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

_(reply depth: 19)_

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-07-26T13:51:38+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3f21df5a_8@news.athenanews.com>`
- **References:** `<vhqqpmeposo77b@corp.supernews.com> <20030724224956.05032.00000446@mb-m26.aol.com> <3f20f8b8.30297531@news.optonline.com> <bfr8vt$4sf$1@panix1.panix.com> <217e491a.0307251447.da13783@posting.google.com>`

```

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:217e491a.0307251447.da13783@posting.google.com...
> docdwarf@panix.com wrote
<snip>
> HTML/CGI Web based systems are a bit more difficult.  There has to be
> several fields in each step to keep the interactions down due to
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Very impressed by this, Richard. I'll look at applying some of the same
principles. Haven't had to do it yet but could be in the near future.

> JavaScript is an option for validation, but I feel shouldn't be
> required.  Make it helpful it it runs, if it isn't enabled then make
> sure the system can still be used.

I consider 2 "phases" to "front ending":

1. Editing - concerns itself with format of input and checking that numeric
fields are numbers and dates are dates.

2. Validation - hits the database and does logical cross checking.

Sometimes the borders become a little blurred, although I can't think of any
current Web applications I have that hit the database from ASP or HTML...(I
prefer to leave this to COBOL in CGI or ASAPI).

I DO use JavaScript for Editing. It is fast and useful. I also invoke my own
numeric checking component from it (String2Num), so I know that by the time
stuff gets to the "back end" there is at least a fighting chance it will
pass validation.

For my users, it is always enabled. (Browser compatibility for "look and
feel" is more problematical for me, than whether JavaScript is enabled...I
try to avoid using Browser dependent HTML, but it is getting to be a
minefield. This subject is on my list of "Things to be addressed" as soon as
I get round to it.)

I experimented with VBScript and JScript but have opted for JavaScript as
being most feature rich and Browser independent.

Pete.
```

```
"LX-i" <LXi0007@Netscape.net> wrote in message
news:vhqi8ubkhjai11@corp.supernews.com...
> Joe Zitzelberger wrote:
<snip>
>
> Having said that, I am guilty of intentionally coding a Perform Thru in
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I don't think Unisys (your environment as I recall) has done so, but check
to see if your compiler has added support yet for the
  EXIT PARAGRAPH/SECTION/PERFORM

sytax (as an extension to its '85 Standard compiler).  This is part of the
2002 COBOL Standard that several vendors have already implemented.  It is
intended to handle EXACTLY this situation without requiring an "...-EXIT"
paragraph or PERFORM THRU.
```

```
I'm convinced that in all things related to Cobol that understandability 
increases in proportion to brevity.  This is just because it is hard to 
write short, simple statements in Cobol that do unusually confusing  
things.

However, as statements in Cobol can be merged together in all manner of 
ways, the increase in their number has a corresponding decrease on 
understandability.

Other languages, awk being an excellent example, do not follow this law.
```

```
incredible...
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ PERFORM...THRU (was Cobol Development Environment)

_(reply depth: 15)_

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-07-26T00:26:50+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3f2122ba_4@news.athenanews.com>`
- **References:** `<vhqi8ubkhjai11@corp.supernews.com> <20030724225616.05032.00000448@mb-m26.aol.com> <vi2383fem6dl4b@corp.supernews.com>`

```
I have struggled to hold my tongue on this subject but can no longer...<G>

Here in 2003 when computer progarmming is an accepted science, and there are
standard solutions for nearly everything, and certainly accepted codes of
best practice, it is easy to forget that such was not always the case.

When I first learned COBOL (1967) there was very little accepted good
practice around and we tended to use what worked, and copied techniques off
each other as we developed them.

I coded PERFORM  THRU exclusively and NEVER performed a single section or a
paragraph.

My code at that time would have looked very much like the examples Daniel
and Eileen are bewailing. (See, I'm confident enough now to admit to it, but
for many years I would never have done so...<G>)

Why did I adopt this approach?

1. It left NO DOUBT as to what the "range" of the PERFORM was.

2. You could add and amend code within the range and not have to worry about
the controlling perform.

3. EVERYBODY (all 8 of us) on that particular IBM site coded that way, and
the professionals at IBM endorsed it.

After a year or two of this, we had a new chum from England come to work on
the installation. He NEVER used PERFORM...THRU. ALWAYS performed a SECTION.

(It is interesting the "cultural differences" in COBOL code. I have noticed
that German COBOLLers code differently from British ones, who code
differently from Antipodean ones, and so on... Since about the mid-eighties
this has become much less pronounced than it was, probably due to a lot more
mobility and communication, and the emergence of "best practices" and the
recognition that different styles are not necessarily "wrong".)

We were all pretty horrified. What if you add another paragraph
inadvertently? It becomes part of the PERFORM range. It isn't EXPLICIT and
it isn't CLEAR.

I remember having heated arguments with Derek (the new chum) about this.(I
was not the gentle, mellowed, mature individual you see today...<G>) and
finally we had a bet over something pretty trivial (as an alternative to
fisticuffs in the car park) where I agreed to try it his way for a month if
I lost and he agreed to give it up if I won.

I lost, and the rest is history...<G>

To this day, I write SECTIONED code and I never PERFORM anything less than a
SECTION, UNLESS it is an in-line PERFORM. I make no apology for this. It
serves me well, and even my OO Methods are SECTIONED.

COBOL has a structure...DIVISION, SECTION, PARAGRAPH, SENTENCE, STATEMENT. I
see no reason to drop any of these levels.

The reason I have posted this is not to get us all involved in another
"style war", but to simply point out that when you are faced with what you
consider to be "bad code", try and remember that the person who wrote it
thought it was OK, and had reasons for doing it that way.

You may not be privy to the circumstances or the background or the training
(or lack of it) that the author had.

If you are a COBOL professional, the only proper approach is: "It is COBOL.
I can deal with it. That's what I do..."

The best way to change people's minds about coding practice is by example.

Pete.









"LX-i" <LXi0007@Netscape.net> wrote in message
news:vi2383fem6dl4b@corp.supernews.com...

crazy too.

…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
```

```
On 25-Jul-2003, docdwarf@panix.com wrote:

> I was taught that this method was superior if paging was a concern in a
> program... hey, anybody remember paging?

Yep.   They were useful then.

And more recently COBOL sorts required sections.    With these, I had a
REST-OF-THE-PROGRAM SECTION.   This isolated the performed paragraphs from the
mandatory sections.   Getting rid of this requirement was a good riddance.

I work in an environment where we have error routines in copymembers with
sections, so I put in a dummy section at the start of my Procedure division to
get rid of the warning.

If shops ever go to the next standard, then there will be no excuse for
procedure division sections nor perform thru's other than "utilizing existing
copymembers".    But I don't know if mainframe shops will be sufficiently
motivated to spend money to go to the next standard.   Meanwhile, I don't need
EXIT PARAGRAPH to write structured code.
```
