###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: PERFORM...THRU (was Cobol Development Environment)

_(reply depth: 17)_

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-07-26T05:47:30+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3f216de6_8@news.athenanews.com>`
- **References:** `<vhqi8ubkhjai11@corp.supernews.com> <20030724225616.05032.00000448@mb-m26.aol.com> <vi2383fem6dl4b@corp.supernews.com> <3f2122ba_4@news.athenanews.com> <bfr99p$6im$1@panix1.panix.com>`

```

<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:bfr99p$6im$1@panix1.panix.com...
> In article <3f2122ba_4@news.athenanews.com>,
> Peter E.C. Dashwood <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> >After a year or two of this, we had a new chum from England come to work
on
> >the installation. He NEVER used PERFORM...THRU. ALWAYS performed a
SECTION.
>
> I was taught that this method was superior if paging was a concern in a
> program... hey, anybody remember paging?

It wasn't "paging", Doc. It was "overlaying". And it was the Programmer's
responsibility. (Paging is the OS's).

We did it until Virtual Storage was invented.

(The Fortress approach was taken regarding Virtual Storage, as we all felt
threatened by it because it meant all the skill we had developed to provide
clever overlay schemes were now redundant. We therefore deliberately ensured
that programs converted to VS performed like tired politicians and
"thrashed" as much as possible. We could then gleefully point out to
Management that our benchmarks showed this "new technology" was a heap of
crap and everything would go down the tubes if it was adopted....All it did
was delay the inevitable but it made us feel better. I giuess things haven't
really changed much in some regards...).

COBOL Sections in the Procedure Division could be followed by a number.
Elsewhere you specified a "segment limit". If the number coded on a given
Section was NOT greater than the segment limit, the section of code remained
resident; if it was greater than the segment limit, it went  into overlay.

I remember being very impressed when I moved to ICL 1900s because they had a
further "grey area" called "quasi-permanent" which was between 50 and the
specified segment limit. These were areas of code that COULD be swapped out
but WOULDN'T be unless absolutely necessary....

Even after the need for overlaying was removed we kept our sections, because
we had come to think of code in terms of functions and grouping things
together. All that happened was the segment numbers disappeared from the
SECTION statement.

There was no "structured programming" paradigm at that time, but the
glimmerings of what would become "modular programming" were beginning to
appear. This was later combined with the structured approach, to lay the
basis of OO.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: PERFORM...THRU (was Cobol Development Environment)

_(reply depth: 18)_

- **From:** "Hugh Candlin" <no@spam.com>
- **Date:** 2003-07-25T18:55:40+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<M2fUa.72627$0v4.4834286@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<vhqi8ubkhjai11@corp.supernews.com> <20030724225616.05032.00000448@mb-m26.aol.com> <vi2383fem6dl4b@corp.supernews.com> <3f2122ba_4@news.athenanews.com> <bfr99p$6im$1@panix1.panix.com> <3f216de6_8@news.athenanews.com>`

```
Peter E.C. Dashwood <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
news:3f216de6_8@news.athenanews.com...
>
> <docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message
news:bfr99p$6im$1@panix1.panix.com...
> > In article <3f2122ba_4@news.athenanews.com>,
> > Peter E.C. Dashwood <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> > >After a year or two of this, we had a new chum from England come to
work
> on
> > >the installation. He NEVER used PERFORM...THRU. ALWAYS performed a
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> responsibility. (Paging is the OS's).

I don't think that that regimentation is totally accurate.

I once had a memorable difference of opinion with a VP
of our hardware vendor regarding the acceptability
of the performance of the operating system.

The OS produced run time stats after each batch job.
"Overhead" was always 70-90% of total runtime.
I questioned this, and got the brush off, persisted,
and eventually determined that the OS was structured
to use a Root area and 2 overlay areas.

The subsequent conflict slowed the machine to a crawl,
yet the VP had the effrontery to tell me that
"Every other customer in the world is satisfied -
 you should be too"!  Could/would you resist the opportunity
to be the only person in the world who was correct about something?

The Root area was used to keep the modules assigned to it
resident at all times.  Overlay Area #1 and Overlay Area #2
were used as swap areas.  Read a File, Write a Record,
Read a Device, Write to a Device - all these functions,
and many others, had separate modules which were paged
in and out on demand.  The 4 named modules, however,
were extremely active, yet they were all assigned to
the Overlay areas through lack of foresight and testing.

Once the structure of the OS was reformulated to allow
reassignment of 2 of the modules to the Root, overhead
plummeted and run times decreased dramatically.

Getting back to your original premise that
"It wasn't "paging", Doc. It was "overlaying".",
well, guess what?  We called it "Segmentation".

With regard to the original topic,
I care deeply about program efficiency.
I use PERFORM....THRU always,
with every Paragraph labeled as a Section.

My programs were the only ones in the company
which graded out at 100% when run through
and Optimizer tool to scan for inefficiencies.
```

- **References:** `<vhqi8ubkhjai11@corp.supernews.com> <20030724225616.05032.00000448@mb-m26.aol.com> <vi2383fem6dl4b@corp.supernews.com> <3f2122ba_4@news.athenanews.com> <bfr99p$6im$1@panix1.panix.com> <3f216de6_8@news.athenanews.com> <M2fUa.72627$0v4.4834286@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`

```
"Hugh Candlin" <no@spam.com> wrote 

> Getting back to your original premise that
> "It wasn't "paging", Doc. It was "overlaying".",
> well, guess what?  We called it "Segmentation".

Segmentation, Overlaying and Paging are quite different things, though
there are some dependancies.

Paging is done using 'pages' which are most often fixed sizes (eg 1k)
and is done by the OS.  These pages may be grouped in segments (eg ICL
VME/B) or need not (eg Atlas). The discarding and reloading of pages
may be complex and usually has little to do with how the program has
been written.

Segmentation is often under control of the programmer, though it may
be indirectly. Usually a SECTION or a group of SECTIONs of the same
'priority' may be made into one Segment.

Overlaying is [usually defined as being] done by the program itself,
or by a run-time or library routine in the program, and is specified
either explicitly or implicitly by the programmer.  A program that is
segmented may, but need not, be overlayed.  It may be implicit in the
SECTION priorities but explicitly disallowed or disabled in the
compiling or linking.

> With regard to the original topic,
> I care deeply about program efficiency.
> I use PERFORM....THRU always,
> with every Paragraph labeled as a Section.
 
When I first started in computers it was as an operator.  One
particular large program took a long time to complete and was
overlayed on tape.  That tape was constantly moving.  The programmer
was doing a PERFORM of the SECTION and the section 'priority' was 60
or so. He felt that as this was the only section being performed at
the time there was no problem (he never watched it run) and what did a
new operator trainee know anyway.

It was overlayed on disk for production runing and hammered the drive.
 Turns out the PERFORM was 'UNTIL End-Of-File' so the overlay was
being reloaded for every READ.

The program was changed to PERFORM the first paragraph of the segment
_once_ and that performed the rest of the segment until end-of-file.

'efficiency' is indepenent of whether it is SECTIONs or THRU.

> My programs were the only ones in the company
> which graded out at 100% when run through
> and Optimizer tool to scan for inefficiencies.

What has that to do with 'PERFORM THRU' or 'PERFORM SECTION' ?
```

```
Those were the days <G>... I used to love watching tape overlaid programs
running. I'm guessing this was a 1900?

The 360s were even more fun because they had all the flashing console
lights. Tape decks had three coloured lights:

1. Yellow (white...)  - My power is on and I am ready.

2. Green - I am selected and transferring data.

3. Red - I am write protected and cannot be written to.

The Operating System  (TOS <there's a name to conjure with...<G>>) also had
all it's control routines on a special deck designated as SYSRES. When a job
finished this deck would go crazy as it loaded more OS transients to handle
JCL. SYSRES was "special" because it could read backwards as well as
forwards. It cost about three times more than the other decks to have this,
but IBM required it...

I remember working the night shift in South Auckland and running the thing
in the dark... Amazing!
```

```
Well Hugh,

we called it "Segmentation" OR "overlaying" (probably because of the
influence of the SEGMENT-LIMIT clause which I described, or maybe the
converse was true, and COBOL adopted SEGMENT-LIMIT to support
segmentation...lost in the mists of time)

It was never "paging" because "paging" hadn't been invented.

Further comments below...
```

```
It was a shop standard to make every paragraph a SECTION
to avoid problems where only some paragraphs were SECTIONS.
This caused havoc when a SECTION qualifier was left out
or accidentally deleted, which could easily happen
when a program was maintained.

They are definitely not mutually exclusive.

Hardware, schmardware.  When I'm on the end of a wire
hundreds or thousands of miles away from the mainframe,
it has mattered little what is bolted to the other end of that wire.
```

```
"Hugh Candlin" <no@spam.com> wrote in message
news:IlmUa.71678$3o3.4816506@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net...
>
<snip>

> > It was never "paging" because "paging" hadn't been invented.
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Er..."paging" was required in order to implement Virtual Storage.

Overlaying (Segmentation, if you like), was around by necessity, BEFORE VS
systems were available. I alluded to using it on an IBM system running TOS
(Tape Operating System). It preceded DOS. It therefore also preceded DOS/VS
(I remember when this first came out, although I couldn't tell you the exact
date.) In fact, it was necessary for the arrival of disk drives to enable
effective paging and give us "Virtual Storage.

At the time COBOL programmers first used Overlaying, paging had not been
invented. It was obsoleted by the advent of paged Virtual Storage systems.


<snip>
> Again, just to be clear, I am talking about paging parts of the OS,
> not the application program.  But paging is paging, right?
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> >

there were people there who remembered it with fond affection.

<snip>

> > > I care deeply about program efficiency.
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…

Thanks for that. I couldn't get my head around using both. If you use
Sections (or single paragraphs, as Richard advocates) there is no need to
use PERFORM...THRU and doing so would be more confusing than helpful, I
would've thought.

You are quite correct that they aren't mutually exclusive as far a s COBOL
is concerned. I had actually forgotten that you CAN say PERFORM Section-1
THRU Section-2. That's because I would never do it...


> >
> > > My programs were the only ones in the company
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> >

No worries. One of the problems with written words is that they sometimes
don't convey the nuances that are available in spoken communication. I am
sometimes amazed when people take exception to stuff I write, where none was
intended. Then I re-read it and realise another, altogether unintended
meaning is possible.

> > Do you still "care deeply about program efficiency"
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>

My comment was intended to be tongue-in-cheek...

The days when we NEEDED to really concern ourselves over efficiency have,
for the most part, been overtaken by quantum leaps (literally...<G>) in the
manufacture of hardware. That doesn't mean we shouldn't consider it, but the
days of burning the midnight oil to squeeze a few milliseconds out of code,
or make something fit into 8K are (apart from some very highly specialized
applications that are not the domain of COBOL) over.

Pete.
```

```
NO.  I do not buy that for a New York second.
You would obviously be surprised at how much time and money
is spent globally trying to squeeze 10 hour jobs down to 2 hours.

> That doesn't mean we shouldn't consider it, but the
> days of burning the midnight oil to squeeze a few milliseconds out of
code,
> or make something fit into 8K are (apart from some very highly

> applications that are not the domain of COBOL) over.

I know for a fact that they are not.  My most recent experience was being
called in on a weekend with every other available resource capable
of coding COBOL to check 1000 online programs and 2000 batch programs
and change every single program in the shop if necessary to ensure that
no unnecessary IMS calls of the Hold variety were being issued.

We also expended a lot of effort to tie sorts to the corresponding
fastpath database structure to make sure that any file being matched
against a fastpath IMS database was sequenced for maximum efficiency.

We also frequently went in and split up jobs by database area so that
the master scheduler could run multiple area-specific jobs concurrently.

Or split jobs in two if that would allow a critical database to be released
significantly sooner and thus allow dependent jobs to execute sooner.

Sorry, but there is no way I will agree on this one.  This is a case where
saving time is more important than the money expended to do so.
```

```
and boring as all get go for everybody else.  But, you cannot please
everyone....

> (We also found that sorting transactions before applying them to fast
path
> databases, helped. Until someone pointed out that the whole idea of using
> databases was to avoid having to sort anything...<spoilsport!>)

A good idea, but that isn't how it works in the real world,
unless you enjoy reading from the start of the database
every time you want to find a record.
>
> But it was 30 years ago.

"But that was yesterday, and yesterday's gone."
>
> I agree that for the point you are at, efficiency is still a major
> consideration.
> (I would just add that I have never suggested that overall efficiency
(like
> run times) does not need to be considered; I was talking about efficiency
at
> the programming level...)

So was I.  Issuing a GU call, followed by a GHU call if necessary, is
programming.
Setting up a database load job's JCL so that it runs in 2 hours instead of
24
may not seem like programming, but it is.
>
> Anyway, you are entitled to your opinion and it is just as valid as
> mine...<G>

I would rather agree with you (or anyone else), but if we happen to
disagree,
in a civil manner, then I find that to be acceptable also.
```

```
> Er..."paging" was required in order to implement Virtual Storage.

Not necessarily.  Burroughs didn't use paging, they swapped unpaged
variable length segments. PCs (eg Windows and Unix/Linux) are
similarly not paged.
 
> date.) In fact, it was necessary for the arrival of disk drives to enable

It was better on drums because they had head-per-track, thus access
time = latency.
 
The first disk drives I worked on (at Ceramic House) were ICL 2802s -
8 Megabyte and 250 ms access time.
```

```
Peter E.C. Dashwood wrote:

> we called it "Segmentation" OR "overlaying" (probably because of the
> influence of the SEGMENT-LIMIT clause which I described, or maybe the
> converse was true, and COBOL adopted SEGMENT-LIMIT to support
> segmentation...lost in the mists of time)

Segmentation is the process of dividing a program up into Segments using 
sections.  Overlaying is arranging for some of those segments to use the 
same memory area(s). While overlaying may require segmentation the reverse 
is not true, they are not synonymous.

> It was never "paging" because "paging" hadn't been invented.

Atlas was doing demand paged memory around 1962.

> Why would you use "SECTION" AND "PERFORM...THRU"? I would've thought they
> are mutually exclusive.

There is technically no problem in doing PERFORM Section-1 THRU Section-2, 
at least as far as the syntax is concerned.  I just can't think of any 
reason why anyone would do that - ensuring that every possible bug trap was 
set with no discernable benefit.
```

```
We still use PERFORM THRU...it's the only thing I've ever seen.  I've
stopped doing this a little when I executed the following code in CICS
transaction

PERFORM P200-BEGIN THRU P200-EXIT


P200-BEGIN.
          ..do stuff.
P300-EXIT.
          EXIT.

The transaction constantly appeared to be executing TWICE.  Someone had done
incomplete paragraph renumbering.  It took a long time to find this because
who ever looked at those 'EXIT' things....so little can go wrong there.

> When I first learned COBOL (1967) there was very little accepted good
> practice around and we tended to use what worked, and copied techniques
off
> each other as we developed them.
Same thing for me exactly (except it was 97).  COBOL...no original code for
35 years!

> To this day, I write SECTIONED code and I never PERFORM anything less than
a
> SECTION, UNLESS it is an in-line PERFORM. I make no apology for this. It
> serves me well, and even my OO Methods are SECTIONED.
Interestingly enough in my time I have NEVER seen SECTION.  Of course I also
hadn't seen local-storage or any other things.  The level of copying in
COBOL is endemic - it's so long winded that I"ve never seen anyone start
without a template.....of course this means that no one know what the heck
the environment division is even for.

> The reason I have posted this is not to get us all involved in another
> "style war", but to simply point out that when you are faced with what you
> consider to be "bad code", try and remember that the person who wrote it
> thought it was OK, and had reasons for doing it that way.
As far as I an concerned - quality is the most underrated style of all.

> If you are a COBOL professional, the only proper approach is: "It is
COBOL.
> I can deal with it. That's what I do..."
> The best way to change people's minds about coding practice is by example.
Or share it on a newsgroup :-)  Seems to me that in this case whilst it is
not a desired style it is seen everywhere.  I like your philosphy of deal
with it and by example (especially if there is still quality).....if you add
*explain* why you think this is better you'd have a great working
environment.  You hiring?

JCE
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: PERFORM...THRU (was Cobol Development Environment)

_(reply depth: 16)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2003-07-25T14:10:04-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0307251310.71a95925@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<vhqi8ubkhjai11@corp.supernews.com> <20030724225616.05032.00000448@mb-m26.aol.com> <vi2383fem6dl4b@corp.supernews.com> <3f2122ba_4@news.athenanews.com>`

```
"Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote 

> I coded PERFORM  THRU exclusively and NEVER performed a single section or
> a paragraph.

> 1. It left NO DOUBT as to what the "range" of the PERFORM was.

Nor does PERFORM paragraph.
 
> He NEVER used PERFORM...THRU. ALWAYS performed a SECTION.

There is only one reason why it is to be a PERFORM SECTION or PERFORM
THRU rather than a PERFORM single-paragraph, and that is to allow
paragraph labels within the scope of the PERFORM.

And the only reason for there to be paragraph labels is to allow for
the use of a GO TO to that paragraph.

If there were no GO TO, then there would be no need of the label. If
there is no label then the THRU or SECTION is not required.  If there
are labels that are not used then why have them ? just comment them
out.

By coding in a style that avoid any GO TO the PERFORMs can be recast
as PERFORM single-paragraph.  This menas that there is then _no_ flow
of control from one paragraph to the next - no drop thru at all.  New
paragraphs can be added anywhere between existing paragraphs. 
Paragraphs can be shifted from one place to anther without any
possibility of logic flow problems.

When working with programs that have PERFORM SECTION or PERFORM THRU
one must be constantly aware of where those boundaries are and ensure
they are maintained, and also keep track of flow in the block of code,
this increases the workload.  By only ever using PERFORM paragraph
that tracking work is eliminated.

A counter argument may be that by using a GO TO to exit this
eliminates deeply nested IFs.  However that is only a result of the
difficulty of creating a new block of code because it would have to be
outside the scope of the current block.  When the restriction on
creating new paragraphs is eliminated and paragraphs can be freely
created without needing to keep track of PERFORM scopes then deep
nesting is removed by PERFORMs, not by GO TOs.

Instead of:

         para1.
             IF ( something )
                 GO TO para1exit.
             IF ( NOT another )
                 GO TO para1exit.
             ....
          para1exit. exit.

we can do:

         para1.
             IF ( NOT something )
                 PERFORM para2
             END-IF
             .
         para2.
             IF ( another )
                 ....
             END-IF
             .

_and_ I don't have to care about just breaking para1 and making a
para2.  This also has the effect of making many IFs simpler.  Rather
than having complex conditions to catch every possibilty because the
action gets written once, I can just break out the action and have
several simple IFs each PERFORMing the new paragraph containing the
action - because I don't care about where that paragraph has to go, it
can follow immediately.

PERFORM THRU and PERFORM SECTION tend (in my experience) to result in
larger scopes because of the effort required to create a new scope. 
This results in new scopes being far removed from their origin,
creating more workload.  This results in a reluctance to put code into
a new scope and thus in larger scopes (which ...).

PERFORM paragraph tends to result in many smaller scopes grouped
together, because you can.

> We were all pretty horrified. What if you add another paragraph
> inadvertently? It becomes part of the PERFORM range. It isn't EXPLICIT and
> it isn't CLEAR.

That's it.
 
> I remember having heated arguments with Derek (the new chum) about this.

PERFORM SECTION and PERFORM THRU are the _same_thing_.  That is
arguing about whether the deck chairs on the Titanic should be facing
outward or forward.

> COBOL has a structure...DIVISION, SECTION, PARAGRAPH, SENTENCE,
> STATEMENT. I see no reason to drop any of these levels.

Wheras I do see a reason (in fact several) for doing so.

Other languages use different types of labels for different purposes. 
C has procedure names (which can't be GO TOed or dropped thru) and
labels (which can't be 'performed' or called).

In Cobol paragraph and section names are labels which can be performed
or gotoed or dropped thru.  This is the cause of the problem (IMHO)
and increases the complexity of programs (which is why you have to
track the -exit paragraphs and section headers).  By adopting a
strategy where labels, _all_ labels, are used for _one_ thing only -
for being the target of a PERFORM and _never_ being dropped thru or
GOTOed the complexity of the program, and of dealing with it, is
reduced enormously.  Labels can be treated as easily as procedure
names in C.

Half arsed attempts at making this change partially do not work (which
is probably why it is seldom found).  It is not until _all_ THRU,
SECTION and GO TO are eliminated that the benefits arise.

> The reason I have posted this is not to get us all involved in another
> "style war", 

Sorry about this.  I am not posting it as a 'style war', only what I
have learnt over 35 years of Cobol and several other language coding.

> but to simply point out that when you are faced with what you
> consider to be "bad code", try and remember that the person who wrote it
> thought it was OK, and had reasons for doing it that way.

I used to code that way, too.  And I did have good reasons for doing
it that way at the time.
```

```
Richard,

I'm glad you posted this again as there are new people here who won't have
seen it.

You make a cogent case for the specialized use of labels, and while I don't
disagree with what you say, I won't be changing.

Not because I'm an obstinate bastard, or firmly believe what I do is the
ONLY way, but because I consider the risks involved in adopting a new
strategy that requires considerable change to my existing practices (which
aren't broken, anyway), is too high.

(Basically, it is too late in the day for me to take this on board.)

However, I think many new people coming in to COBOL would do well to
consider your arguments.

There are a few quick comments below, but overall, I don't disagree with you
on this.

Pete.

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:217e491a.0307251310.71a95925@posting.google.com...
> "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote

It didn't  (and doesn't...) EXPLICITLY state what the end of the range is.

>
> > He NEVER used PERFORM...THRU. ALWAYS performed a SECTION.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…

Weeellll...., not entirely (at least not in my case...)

I consider the aesthetic use of using SECTIONS to group related paragraphs
together, to be valuable and in line with the overall aesthetic of COBOL (as
opposed to other languages). I DO have unreferenced paragraphs within my
sections (sometimes, not always) and the paragraphs are intended to be
documenting, rather than points for GO TO. It goes back again to the old IBM
days when we used READY TRACE and the system spat out the flow of the
program through paragraphs. It made sense to use meaningful paragraph names
as a method of documenting the flow. These days I keep sections small
(especially OO Methods) and I COULD use a single paragraph as you suggest. I
do sometimes exit a SECTION by the single exit point (just as you described,
and I accept your arguments against the need for this. It comes down to
habit; I don't really need to think about it.
(Habit: can be "good", can be "bad"... It's like I have only ever coded
PERFORM...WITH TEST AFTER once in my life. It was a very special case
involving API access and it made sense to change the PERFORM in this way,
rather than do all the other things I would've had to do to make it work.
Yes, I understand the the difference between DO...WHILE and DO...UNTIL, but
this is COBOL. As an old hand, I KNOW that PERFORM tests before and I always
use ">" to take this into account (where the loop is controlled by a count,
obviously <G>). Whether it is a good or bad habit, as long as I apply it
consistently, it works for me.).

> And the only reason for there to be paragraph labels is to allow for
> the use of a GO TO to that paragraph.
>

> out.
>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: PERFORM...THRU (was Cobol Development Environment)

_(reply depth: 19)_

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-07-30T15:35:38+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
"Harley" <dennis.harleyNoSpam@worldnet.att.net> wrote in message
news:xZCVa.79687$0v4.5277990@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net...

> | strategy that requires considerable change to my existing practices
(which
> | aren't broken, anyway), is too high.
> |
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Isn't this the Fortress Mentality in action?

Touche <G>!

Given the reasons I outlined (risks vs rewards), given that I have not
dismissed it out of hand or evaluated it through the spectacles of my
preference and prejudice, given that I no longer have a career in computer
programming and therefore don't need to consider carefully changes to
current practice, I would argue that my response is a long way from the
Fortress Mentality.

Questions of style are not what the Fortress is about. The Fortress is about
resisting changes in PRACTICE. This is NOT just about COBOL programming
(although Programmers will see it from that perspective); it is about the
whole way in which we design and develop computer Applications.

>
> P.S. I agree with Richard.

So do I, but not to the extent that I am prepared to introduce major change
into everything I currently do...<G>.

Fortunately, I can afford that luxury as I am responsible for what I do and
no-one else has to maintain my code. If that were not the case, and if I
expected to be spending another ten years as a commercial COBOL programmer,
my response would certainly be different.

> I use neither Sections, nor THRUs, and GO TO is used only for the ABEND
> routine.
> It's a matter of personal preference, I'm not a Nazi about it.

So you agree it is style and you are not Evangelizing it? Good.

> I get to do quite a bit of new development, and it makes things simpler
for
> me.
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Structured Programming is certainly a "Good Thing".

OO Programming is better. (although this doesn't obviate the need for
structure, it would be foolish to build large integrated OO Methods).
Encapsulation enforces a certain structure.

Component building is as good as it gets for me..."The Keys to the Kingdom
<G>". I do it for fun... and nobody has to worry about what style of code I
use...

Pete.
```

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:


>PERFORM SECTION and PERFORM THRU are the _same_thing_.  That is
>arguing about whether the deck chairs on the Titanic should be facing
>outward or forward.

>In Cobol paragraph and section names are labels which can be performed
>or gotoed or dropped thru.  This is the cause of the problem (IMHO)
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>SECTION and GO TO are eliminated that the benefits arise.

I second Richard's comments.  If you don't use GO TO, there is no reason for
SECTION or THRU. If you feel the need for GO TO, redo your structure or consider
EVALUATE .. CONTINUE.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: PERFORM...THRU (was Cobol Development Environment)

_(reply depth: 18)_

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-07-26T21:36:19+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3f22f296.159818991@news.optonline.com>`
- **References:** `<vhqi8ubkhjai11@corp.supernews.com> <20030724225616.05032.00000448@mb-m26.aol.com> <vi2383fem6dl4b@corp.supernews.com> <3f2122ba_4@news.athenanews.com> <217e491a.0307251310.71a95925@posting.google.com> <3f22201b.105927807@news.optonline.com>`

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote:

> If you feel the need for GO TO, redo your structure or consider
>EVALUATE .. CONTINUE.

Here is an example.

--- Before ---

 if  a
     go to the-exit.
 if  b
     go to the-exit.
 if  c
     perform p1
 else
 if  d
     perform p2
 end-if
 end-if
 move x to y.
the-exit.
 exit.

-- After --

evaluate true
    when a
    when b
        continue
    when other
        evaluate true
            when c
                perform p1
            when d
                perform p2
        end-evaluate
        move x to y
 end-evaluate.
```

```
"LX-i" <LXi0007@Netscape.net> wrote in message
```

```
> > serves me well, and even my OO Methods are SECTIONED.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
There ARE no "between sections" in my code. And yes, SECTIONS are COBOL's
way of grouping activities relating to a common function.

The reasons I do it are outlined in my response to Richard.

For me, it is a combination of past history and COBOL aesthetics. I do not
advocate that everyone should do it; in fact I agree with Richard and Robert
that there is no need for it if you consider ONLY the logical requirements
and NEVER use GOTO.

(I do that as well <G>, but with the "nicest possible taste..." as the late,
great Kenny Everett would say...<G>. I limit my GO TOs to a single entry or
exit point, but I agree that this can be avoided if you REALLY care about
pure programming. I was programming BEFORE it was pure, BEFORE there were
accepted best practices, BEFORE there were Computer Science courses, so I
have acquired some "bad" habits. Nevertheless, my code works and no-one has
ever complained about maintaining it. I can also produce it pretty quickly.

Hence, I make no apology for it.

Pete.

you
```

- **From:** "Kevin J. Hansen (Norcom)" <khansen@screenio.com>
- **Date:** 2003-07-17T01:49:46+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a8vbhvok0n1ntlngktrp7bab30v3m0da1n@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3118241.1058378028@dbforums.com> <bf46lr$8ul$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3118793.1058387352@dbforums.com>`

```
On Wed, 16 Jul 2003 20:29:12 +0000, hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com>
wrote:

<snip>

> And the real shame  is that these programmers are ones who
>know and understand the company's business and are far superior than
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>mainframe"...
---------------
Howard, you certainly hit the nail on the head.  It seems every recent
college grad who's taken a C++, VB, or Delphi class thinks he/she's a
DP professional.  Ditto for the self-taught programmers.

Knowing how to -write- a program is not at all the same as -designing-
a good user interface, a robust file structure, or an application
that's fault-tolerant...  As you observed, that's where the value of
the Old Guard lies.

The question isn't mainframe/PC, it's simply a matter of using the
appropriate tool for the job.  When I first heard 20 years ago that
COBOL ran on the (original) IBM PC, I just laughed - I "knew" there
was no way it could be done.

A year later, though, we were making a very nice living marketing PC
COBOL developer tools and porting online applications from the
mainframe platform to PC networks.  We adapted and thrived.

PCs are here to stay, and should be treated simply as another tool
available to us.  The fact that mainframe COBOL business logic can be
moved to the PC platform just makes it easier to take advantage of the
technology that's available on PCs.  Not to say that everything should
move - there'll always be a place for mainframes - but, some stuff is
worth moving to a more suitable platform in order to take advantage of
what's there.

Just my $0.02 worth.

Kevin
Kevin J. Hansen
GUI ScreenIO - Windows(tm) Programming in COBOL!
http://www.ScreenIO.com
```

```
But Kevin there is no such thing as a robust file system anymore.
There is no VSAM, there is x'0d0a' delimited text file that functions
as sequential access. There is no BDAM, there is no QSAM, there is no
DSORG=PO. What's left is arrays and relational data bases - most of
which haven't even been analyzed for first form normalization.
Access, FoxPro, MS Sql, Oracle, Sybase, Filemaker they not only define
the file access but also have accountant level interfaces to design
spread sheets and basic record/row retrievals. The heck of it is most
people are satisfied with the level of programming support provided by
the DB vendors.

It sort of reminds me of the Easytrieve, DYL260/280 panacea.
Furthermore, if there is a design architecture to be done it will occur
in India more than likely. Rapidly we American programmers are being
replaced overseas.
```

```
Something to that, but even if you're using a database package, a

Roger re Easytrieve, but it did the job...  If the only tool you have
is a hammer, you know.

As for the offshore remark, that's been happening in other fields for
years; look at machine tools, for example.  A lot of why we won in
WWII was that we had a lot of basic industry here.  These days, most
stuff is built offshore (with the exception of Hardinge and some other
really high-end stuff).  If you wanted to be paranoid, consider what
would happen if we were unable to import heavy machinery when we
needed it, a concern of some of my friends.

As long as we're not willing to work for $20/hour, work is going to
move offshore.  But, that doesn't prevent us from innovating in other
ways.  It's a natural progression.  Is that bad?  No easy answer.

Kevin
```

```
So you have worked at mainframe companies that bought software and then did
not have the resources to run it. This is simply a management problem and
not a platform problem.

You claim that the PC platform has many, many advantages over the mainframe
platform and from the kindness in your heart you want to help others see
the light. From that one would think that you would go on and on covering
the PC advantages. Yet instead of that, you begin attacking and criticising
mainframes and citing past experiences which are diametrically opposed to
my experiences. Would you try to woo a woman by attacking her deficiencies.
This is nothing but prejudice, clear, plain and simple.
People with your attitude who express that attitude strongly are actually
doing more harm than good. They piss off mainframe people who then dismisss
their arguments as so much maiframe bashing. Valid arguments that need to
be heard are not listened to.

FWIW I am for learning all I can about both environments. I bought Chris
Richardson's book and your CDs on NetCOBOL for .NET. So I am not pro
mainfrane and anti PC, yet your attitude really turns me off. How do you
think less neutral, more opinionated people will react to you. If helping
people is your true goal then that is admirable but I for one find your
approach terribly flawed. Perhaps if I knew you personally I would
recognize something in your postings that I am currently missing that would
cause me to view them differently.

technically advanced PC platform.  I have now tried them on five different
PCs without any success.  On each one there has been a different technical
glitch that stopped the playback. Gee if the were only sequential QSAM
files they could be read on any mainframe made in the last 40 years or so.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-07-17T12:45:45+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3f15f259_2@news.athenanews.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3118241.1058378028@dbforums.com> <bf46lr$8ul$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:bf46lr$8ul$1@peabody.colorado.edu...
>
> On 16-Jul-2003, hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com> wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> survive.   Where I have worked, COBOL has been on the mainframe, if we
want to
> work on PCs, we switch tools.   The hard part is to get companies to pay
> attention to their promises to give their valuable COBOL programmers
experience
> with the new tools.

This is an area where the Doc and I hold different views, and I suspect that
your affinity would be closer to his position than to mine. (I don't think
either of you are "wrong"; I just believe that the responsibility rests with
the individual.)

Why can programmers only get experience with new tools if the Company pay
for it?

Companies break promises all the time. So do people. We deal with it.

If I had waited for Companies to honour their promises to me, I would still
be writing Fortress COBOL and bewailing the state of the Job market.

I know there is a valid case that Companies SHOULD motivate employees with
additional training and that they SHOULD pay for it, and  that they SHOULD
ensure the opportunity is available for people to get training, but what are
you going to do?

Sit around and wait for it to happen? (Then find many years down the track
that you are hopelessly out of touch with the technology because you never
got any of the things you were promised, and now your maket value is
severely slashed) or take responsibility for your own professional
development ( and it doesn't do your personal development any harm either,
to learn new things), get off your arse and accept that YOU are responsible
for the condition YOU are in. Not the Company, not the wife and kids, not
the in-laws, not the Government...

Some managers and companies DO take promises very seriously (I am one) and
try to ensure that what is promised is delivered, but sadly, they are in the
minority.

So, given that the world is an imperfect place, what should you do about it?

Here's my advice:

1. Set up a training plan for yourself and decide the things you need to
learn within the next year.

2. Run it past your Management and see if they are prepared to buy in to it.
(They may not be able to justify some of it but even if they support you in
principle, it's a start.)

3. If they are not, start looking for another job. (If nothing else, this
will enable you to assess what your market value is.)

4. Decide what you can reasonably fit into a program of home study and do
it. (There are dozens of reasons to doubt how effective this will be...put
them all aside and just do it.)

5. If you have access to documentation where you work, take advantage of the
opportunity.

6. Read, learn, apply where you can and have the opportunity. In other
words, increase your value.

At the end of the year, assess what you learned. If you can't use it where
you are, then definitiely go somewhere you can.

If there isn't anywhere, keep looking until there is.

Above all, keep your own program moving. NEVER rely on others to move it for
you!

Pete.
```

```
In article <3f15f259_2@news.athenanews.com>,

>for it?

Mr Dashwood, I have told you before... this is not my view.  My attempts 
to point out to you that this is not my view have seemed to be futile; you 
appear to have it in your head that you know better what it is that my 
view is than I do and you insist on misrepresenting it.

>
>Companies break promises all the time. So do people. We deal with it.

It was never suggested otherwise, Mr Dashwood, at least by me.

>
>If I had waited for Companies to honour their promises to me, I would still
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>you going to do?

Whatever it is that 'you' are comfortable with, Mr Dashwood... coercion 
can be an unpleasant phenomenon.

[snip]

>Here's my advice:
>
>1. Set up a training plan for yourself and decide the things you need to
>learn within the next year.

I agree with this advice.

>
>2. Run it past your Management and see if they are prepared to buy in to it.
>(They may not be able to justify some of it but even if they support you in
>principle, it's a start.)

I do not necessarily agree with this advice; I would say that the 
atmosphere of the workplace is a much better indicator as to how your 
initiatives will be received.  (see above about 'broken promises')

>
>3. If they are not, start looking for another job. (If nothing else, this
>will enable you to assess what your market value is.)

I agree with this advice but in a more general fashion... be as prepared 
to find a new job as your employer seems to be prepared to put people in a 
position where finding a new job seems to be a Good Idea.  Reciprocation.

[snip]

>Above all, keep your own program moving. NEVER rely on others to move it for
>you!

Above all, do not allow your newly-gained skills to be taken advantage of 
without appropriate recompense.  Never mind that the ability to learn new 
skills is, I would say, more valuable than the individual skills... if you 
are doing something new for your employer which garners that employer a 
profit then - within the bounds of reason, goodwill and amity that exist 
in your workplace - you should be garnering a profit as well.

You Do More For Them - They Do More For You.  *This* is my belief, Mr 
Dashwood.  The goal of Doing Business should *not* be, in my view, 'How 
much can I get for giving how little?' but 'How do all parties involved 
show a benefit from these interactions?'

DD
```

```
Table for two, window view... no, this bit of paranoia on my part is based
on experience; the last job I held as an employee was one where I had my
very own IBM PC-AT to work on; it had a copy of Micro Focus COBOL (the V
1.7something I've mentioned earlier) and was networked to a Bernouilli
box.  This was so wonderful compared to the Kaypron 8088 with NEC V20
speedup-chip that I had at home that I came in on a Saturday just to play
around with it.  As I was pounding away my supervisor came in; he was 
surprised to see me and we traded a few pleasantries before getting to our 
separate tasks.

On Monday I heard from one of his favorites that he (the supervisor) no 
longer considered me to be trustworthy; he just *knew* that I had come in 
on Saturday to work on my resume.

Conclusion: if my mere presence for play-purposes could be misinterpreted 
in that manner I would be cautious about informing a supervisor about 
getting new skills; it might also be concluded that one is about to Take 
A Hike and one's position and treatment might grow less pleasant as a 
result.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>

- **Message-ID:** `<bf46r8$sbt$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
Howard,
  Just to give you an ALTERNATE possibility.

My personal opinion is that the problem is that Micro Focus and Fujitsu have
been trying (for the last 15-20 years) to "move COBOL off the mainframe"
RATHER than addressing the needs and "desires" of COBOL where it is "already
native and thriving".

I think that COBOL has *minimal* future "on the workstation" (not none, just
minimal).  I think its future is in changing to meet the changing needs of
the mainframe - and YES, mainframes are "here to stay".
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2003-07-16T19:41:49+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bf49pu$abt$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3118241.1058378028@dbforums.com> <bf46r8$sbt$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net>`

```

On 16-Jul-2003, "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote:

>   Just to give you an ALTERNATE possibility.
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> the mainframe - and YES, mainframes are "here to stay".

That makes sense.   The mainframe is changing as well, as mainframes, minis, PCs
will all be seen as parts of the big picture.   The problems will be in how to
control the interfaces so that security needs, privacy needs, and accessibility
needs are met.    OO may be how various tools communicate, but the objects may
need to be under the control of a DBA who manages the above needs.

Every time we get a new tool, we have people saying the old tool is obsolete -
and people who explain why the new tool is the final answer.   But it doesn't
take much looking to see that there are needs that are not yet being met.

It isn't obvious to me what Micro Focus and Fujitsu could be doing to address
the needs and desires of COBOL on the mainframe.   Maybe they could fit in a
while as third party sort utilities did until IBM and other big Iron companies
decide to be competitive.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com>
- **Date:** 2003-07-16T20:21:03+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3118788.1058386863@dbforums.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3118241.1058378028@dbforums.com> <bf46r8$sbt$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net>`

```

Bill,
   I can both agree and disagree with you in some regards. I do think it
   is a rather foolish idea to expect Micro Focus or Fujitsu to begin
   developing mainframe COBOL compilers if that's what you are
   suggesting. Fujitsu, incidnetally, already has a mainframe OS/VS
   compiler as I understand (the only otrher such one in existance I'm
   told) - and it was this compiler that was used as a basis for the
   Fujitsu Unix, Linux and PC compilers. This is why there is in fact a
   very high degree of mainframe compatibility in the Fujitsu compiler,
   by the way, even though you choose to disagree.
    The mainframe is pretty much a dead new development platform.
    Especially when you consider COBOL. And the reasons for this are
    quite strong - lack of GUI, lack of interactiveness (my own term).
    Yes there is still development going on on mainframes, but the
    emphasis in many examples is trying to turn the mainframe into
    something the PC already is - look at IBM's websphere for a good
    example of such.
    And mainframes will be with us for a long time still, but primarily
    because of the influence of IBM in IT, and the fear factor induced
    by the idea of movng away from them. But I believe you know yourself
    that the mainframe is dying a slow death just like COBOL has been
    doing. Yes, IBM claims this not to be true, but then there's a
    slight conflict of interest there, isn't there...? And who was IBM's
    chief mainframe competitor in past years...? Could it have been
    say.... FUJITSU?! (stated with a smile on my face while thinking of
    the SNL Chuch Lady saying "SATAN?!"). But Fujitsu is now
    concertrating on parallel servers and such and producing new
    technology .NET COBOL compilers, instead of carrying along a kitchen
    sink like product, aren't they? So it's a rather interesting battle
    shaping up I think.
    And as an ex-Mainframer myself, I don't feel shy about stating the
    above. The bottom line is that the entire development world is
    shifting off of mainframes and so the only question left is how
    long will these hugely expensive less functional boxes continue to
    be put up with by IT departments (or more importantly by CEO's and
    CFO's when they realize the conflict of interest that often exists
    in IT)...?
Thanks,
-Howard
```

```
>     And the reasons for this are
>     quite strong - lack of GUI, lack of interactiveness (my own term).

Mainframes are servers, GUIs are for client machines.  The bridge is
made using web servers and CGI, or similar.

>     Yes there is still development going on on mainframes, but the
>     emphasis in many examples is trying to turn the mainframe into
>     something the PC already is - look at IBM's websphere for a good
>     example of such.

Just exactly what is your idea of what a 'PC' is ?  You seem to use it
as 'any box that is not running OS/VS'.  WebSphere started on AIX,
HP-UX and Sun machines, these are _not_ 'PC's.   While it also runs on
NT, this is Windows trying to be a Unix system.

>     And mainframes will be with us for a long time still, but primarily
>     because of the influence of IBM in IT, and the fear factor induced
>     by the idea of movng away from them. But I believe you know yourself
>     that the mainframe is dying a slow death 

Actually IBM sold more mainframes last year than in any previous year.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2003-07-16T15:21:07-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0307161421.2bf135d0@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3118241.1058378028@dbforums.com>`

```
hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com> wrote 

> The real point I'm trying make is that people sometimes
> attack what they don't understand and in terms of COBOL in particular, I
> believe that the only chance for COBOL's future is to get it off the
> mainframe in most instances and onto PC platforms 

I've been developing Cobol system exclusively on 'PC' and Unix micros
for 25 years.  But I don't believe that dumping mainframes and
abandoning Cobol on mainframes is 'Cobol's saviour'.

Mainframes and PCs do different jobs, just as Mack trucks and pickups
do different jobs. The way PCs work is different from the way
mainframes work. While one _may_ save money by rehosting existing
Cobol code onto Unix, it may require several machines and increased
support costs.

> where software and tools are much richer and far more modern. 

Is this for the software developers ? or for the users ?  

> The mainframe has become  and anchor tied around COBOL's neck, 

Anchors stop things drifting away. 

> and the refusal by many to fully
> embrace the new PC platforms with their COBOL applications simply spells
> doom for them in the future. 

Doom (or not) for the mainframe applications is indendant of whether
the code is rehosted onto a different machine or not.

Moving a Batch/CICS system to a group of PCs doesn't make it anything
other than a Batch/CICS system.

> If they are not migrated, then they will
> simply be replaced by other technologies over time. 

Making a _PC system_ from a Batch/CICS one is not 'migration.', it is
replacement.

> And so I must admit
> I get more than a bit agitated when I seem someone posting a note
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> entrenched way of thinking....

Sure, rehost mainframe apps onto cheaper boxes if there is a business
case for doing that.  The 'cheaper box' may be a modern small sized
'mainframe' or a Unix box or a multi-CPU 'PC'.

But, as a question, just who are you aiming this at ?  Mainframe
programmers aren't going to rush out and buy PCs and compilers to
rehost their employer's code.  That is an executive decision.  The
executives aren't Cobol programmers, they don't care one way or
another whether Cobol programmers keep their jobs or not.

Ranting on about 'refusing to fully embrace' your _opinions_ is
misdirected.
```

```
You know the fact we are all missing is that significant programming
jobs are going off shore. So whether it's COBOL, or C, or C#, or Java,or
PL/I, or whatever.  Whether it's Oracle, or Sun, or DB/2, or MS-SQL, or
Sybase, and again or whatever, we Americans are being deprived of
programming and analysis jobs.

I read that the average fully absorbed cost to a US company for an
Indian with a bona fide master degree is less than $20 in New Dehli. The
$20 is on average $40 per hour cheaper than the median $60/hour cost of
a fully absorded (benefits, office space, social security, light, heat,
airco, etc) cost for an American programmer in America.

With the advent of T1/T3/DSL communications, massive amounts of data
can be exchanged with India - efficiently/proficiently. Maintenance
programming in particular is being hit hard by the off shore
programming option.

The article further stated that by 2005 almost 70% of typical IT
programming and analysis will be off shore. The article speculated that
the MVS system programming groups and the NetWork geeks would be
basically immune from the off shore movement. These guys/gals prsumably
are ons-site/on-call.

So maybe instead of COBOL or Pascal as a language we might want to
consider Hindi or Punjabi?
```

#### ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com>
- **Date:** 2003-07-17T02:37:47+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```

Well Gary, now that you've made sure I've seen your reply.... :-)
I don't know the entire story, but I've been told that Fujitsu built an
MVS compatible (comparable?) operating system for their mainframes some
years back anf IBM got into litigation with them over it. I really don't
know the details of all of this, but maybe someone else on the forum
does. I was additionally told that they had an OS/VS Compatible compiler
on this system and this was the compiler base they used to create their
UNIX, LINUX and Windows compilers. I have always been surprised by the
level of IBM COBOL support in the Fujitsu compiler. There are even
undocumented compiler directives that produce mainframe like things that
we've found purly by accident. For example, there is a completely
undocumented "MAP" directive that produces a DMAP listing.
Thanks,
-Howard
```

```
The bottom-Line (without looking at the news item) is that there was a law
suit due to a "de-coupling" of IBM and Fujitsu where Fujitsu got SOME "then
current" IBM source code.  This included the VS COBOL II compiler which had
the '85 Standard support and significant (but FAR from complete)
compatibility with OS/VS COBOL.
```

#### ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com>

```

My comments regarding mnainframe debuggers weren't clearly understood by
all. I wasn't saying they are never used. What I am saying is that it
was my experience that they either weren't allowed to be used or were
seldom used. And the reason for this is far more complex than people
simply not realizing they had these products (and yes I've heard of
Innertest - Ever heard of Expeditor - probably the best of the bunch,
incidentally - I think someone esle mentioned it as "ADS" which is
actually the name of the company thatmade it). And there is a very good
reason that debuggers are in such low usage on mainframes - because of
the resources they require - especially the way many of them used to
hang VTAM sessions while waiting for interaction frm the user.
Thanks,
-Howard
```

```
I remember back when I had been programming only about two years. This was
the days of punched cards which a courier took about twelve miles over to
the computer room. We got one run per day. Needless to say we desk checked
the hell out of our programs.  One day I heard two people talking about
getting an 0C7 and I asked what that was and they said it was a data
exception. I had to ask them to explain what that was and how it occurred.
In two years I had been so careful in following all the rules that I had
been taught that I had never experienced that problem. Now whenever I have
that problem I think back and realize I am not what I once was.
```

```
In article <3f16bd88.262941348@news.optonline.com>,
 robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote:

> "Harley" <dennis.harleyNoSpam@worldnet.att.net> wrote:
> 
…[36 more quoted lines elided]…
> Robert "Close To The Metal" Wagner

I can provide a cute story about that.

There was a monthly production job that would occasionally notice that 
it was starting to run low on space and might need some more for the 
next months run.  It was written to trigger a loud banner message when 
this condition occurred and abend -- just so someone could notice and 
add some extra space.

(fyi, I don't mean space in the DASD sense, more in the hash table reorg 
sense)

No this condition only occurred when a given client would have crossed a  
2,500,000 account boundary.  And a pad of 2.5 million was always 
available to all clients.  So this was really just a simple warning to 
the support people to add a new 2.5 million chunk sometime in the next 
month.

However -- there were dozens of clients.  So in a given week of on-call 
duty it was almost certain that the on-call person would get called 
(cost to company, 2 hours pay for programmer) and the operators would be 
disturbed from the usual operator duties to babysit the abend and get 
the on-call programmer looking at it.

When TCP/IP and SMTP became available at this shop I suggested that the 
abend and banner message ought to be replaced with a simple email to the 
product team leader.  

The idea was rejected because the current method "worked".  Besides, 
they were still looking at that new TCP/IP thing.  And email, though 
ubiquitous on the LAN was voodoo magic on the mainframe.

Heads in the sand...
```

#### ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

- **From:** hhinman <member30421@dbforums.com>
- **Date:** 2003-07-20T21:57:41+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3132043.1058738261@dbforums.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com>`

```

.. (I really hate the tab key + enter option having submitted posts
before they are done...!)

anyway, continuing the COBOL/2 discussion, Micro Focus  had another
motivation for Equipping IBM and Microsoft with their COBOL/2 - this was
to effectively remove IBM's own PC COBOL compiler from the market which
Microsoft had previously licensed from IBM and  was selling as MS COBOL
as well (or was it the other way around...? - I'm sure Gary will know
and correct me... :-) )
    But in doing this, Micro Focus played a somewhat dangerous game in
    my opinion. Both IBM and Microsoft realized that the COBOL/2
    compiler was lacking in certain functionality for mainframe
    compatibility, and as I understand, both pressured Micro Focus
    heavily for something better. But Micro Focus was not about to give
    up it's Workbench market niche to either of them. So finally, Micro
    Focus signed a distribution contract with IBM for their COBOL
    Workbench and related tools and IBM could go sell it directly - but
    only at a discount and Micro Focus made near the same amount off of
    any sales given their traditional discounts, as I understand. This
    option was either never offered to Microsoft or they refused, I'm
    not sure. But the result was Microsoft pretty much got out of the
    COBOL business and began olely pushing VB and C++ as a replacement
    many claim. And there's even an argument here - that Microsoft were
    never really in the COBOL business and instead were trying  to use
    COBOL as the ticket at the time to get into IS and sell their other
    products. This has changed dramitically in any regard with their
    .NET environment.
   And just to add to the fun, during this time (late 1980's, early
   1990's), Sun Microsystems, who also oemed a Micro Focus COBOL
   compiler, put a great deal of pressure on Micro Focus to OEM the
   Workbench product, as I understand. But Micro Focus never gave it to
   them - Only IBM had the privaledge of distributing Workbench and
   associated components, as I understand. There were a few exceptions
   to this later, but none of them are significant in my opinion.
    And I think another missing point in Bill's note (and as he stated,
    some of this predated his arrival there), is that Micro Focus made a
    big bet on Object COBOL taking off in the market place. VISOC was
    actually a lower priced Object COBOL enabled environment (Micro
    Focus had a second add-on Object COBOL enablement for Workbench at
    the same time). The thought process behind OO COBOL was simply to
    follow the "C" model - most "C" programmers later became C++
    programmers, and so most COBOL porgrammers would naturally become OO
    COBOL programmers - and there were far more COBOL programmers in
    existance at the time than there had been "C" programmers to begin
    the C++ market, so Micro Focus would be selling OO COBOL compilers
    left and right....
   This never materialized, and the OO COBOL market became a bust. It is
   my personal opinion that .NET is the first compelling reason to
   actually learn OO COBOL, but we will see things work out.
    A lot of money was put into VISOC (Visual Object COBOL) -
    particularly in advertising as I remember. I believe that VISOC was
    a $500 product. It is my understanding that VISOC was pulled from
    the marketplace by Micro Focus, and that the newer NET Express
    product was kind of a VISOC Version 2 - using some of the same
    components and having a similar interface, although this is
    speculation on some people's parts.
    Another missing piece was the later acquisition of Burl's REVOLVE
    product which I think occurred around 1995 (Gary - please correct
    me). REVOLVE actually pulled the emphasis in development away from
    PC production systems and back onto mainframe offload because of the
    upcoming Year 2000 "crisis". And Micro Focus made a lot of money off
    of this product strategy, which is something I warned about before
    leaving - that they were mortgaging their future by placing such a
    strong emphasis on mainframe development offloading and Y2K and
    should instead be placing an equal emphasis on building better tools
    to allow users to port mainframe applications to PC's  for
    production.
    I'm sorry to say (and I know I'll get jumped on for this comment..),
    but right after Y2K Micro Focus took a nose dive and I was correct
    in my predictioon of such. People can blame this on  things like the
    Intersolv acquisition - which certainly had an additional negative
    impact on Micro Focus, but the bottom line is that (in  my opinion)
    Micro Focus failed to produce the same kinf of great tools for
    mainframe migration as it had for mainframe development offloading
    and the simple truth is if you don't build software that customers
    want, they are not going to purchase much of it. And I think that's
    what happened at Micro Focus.
    I will state that I was very pleased to see Ian get up at COBOL
    Expo last year and state that this was going to change. And as
    I've said in the past, there are still a few very clever people
    there in development and even elsewhere in the organization
    (including Gary...). So we will see what happens. I think they've
    dug a very deep hole for themselves and this is compounded by the
    downturn in the COBOL market, and the arrival of Fujitsu on the
    scene with an emphaisis on production, but we'll wait and see. I
    wish them the best of luck, because a lot of people feel that
    COBOL in a way rides on Micro Focus' back because of the immense
    market share they enjoyed some 10 years ago... Adn COBOL needs
    multiple vendors with good products in this area to help stimulate
    the market in any regard.
Regards,
-Howard
```

```
Another piece of the puzzle; I think it was the late '80's when Marc
Sokol (who was Mr. Realia at the time) told me that Microsoft was
negotiating with them to buy Realia (and hence Realia COBOL).  Things
were going along well, but Marc wanted -waaaaay- too much so Microsoft
figured he wasn't seriously interested and walked away.  I bet he
still kicks himself over this.

Microsoft obviously wanted to be in the COBOL market but also wanted
to make money at it (Duh!).  When they couldn't find a way to make
money on COBOL (because what they were marketing wasn't competitive
technically with the rest of the marketplace), they threw their weight
behind more profitable developer products; C, C++, and VB.  

One has to wonder what the world would be like if Microsoft had
managed to bring a really good COBOL compiler to the marketplace...

Kevin

Kevin J. Hansen
GUI ScreenIO - Windows(tm) Programming in COBOL!
http://www.ScreenIO.com
```

```
"Kevin J. Hansen (Norcom)" <khansen@screenio.com> wrote 

> Microsoft obviously wanted to be in the COBOL market but also wanted
> to make money at it (Duh!).  When they couldn't find a way to make
> money on COBOL (because what they were marketing wasn't competitive
> technically with the rest of the marketplace), they threw their weight
> behind more profitable developer products; C, C++, and VB.  

I am not sure why you think that MS Cobol 5.0 was not 'competitive
technically' with MicroFocus 3.0, after all it was a version of MF, or
why MF 3.0 (and thus MS 5.0) was not competitive with the rest of the
market.

Certainly MS 5.0 had dropped OS/2 and was on 16 bit, but this was
1992/3. I never liked MS's PWB, but then I disliked MF WB too (I have
yet to meet an IDE that I liked and didn't just get in the way).

MS was certainly working on 5.1 (= MF 3.1) and had promised a copy for
me, but from MS they said that MF had pulled the agreement as they had
opened an office in the US and wanted to direct sell.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol development environment

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Kevin J. Hansen (Norcom)" <khansen@screenio.com>
- **Date:** 2003-07-21T07:44:48+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<lq4nhvo2mr9bcmfpadk95163ui0udq2oh6@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<bda4ue$45j$04$1@news.t-online.com> <3132043.1058738261@dbforums.com> <vq6mhvgc5edjuditr06r84km3vn21iirbj@4ax.com> <217e491a.0307201954.cb887c6@posting.google.com>`

```
On 20 Jul 2003 20:54:22 -0700, riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>I am not sure why you think that MS Cobol 5.0 was not 'competitive
>technically' with MicroFocus 3.0, after all it was a version of MF, or
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>opened an office in the US and wanted to direct sell.
----------
Richard,

As soon as I sent it, I realized that my remark could be interpreted
as criticism of MicroFocus, which was not my intention.  

I just seem to recall that the MS compiler didn't measure up to the
other guys' products when we looked at them.  All of the PC COBOL
compiler vendors supplied us with current versions of their compilers
during this period because there was a lot of demand for us to port
our DOS screen manager to their compilers.  My (admittedly dim)
recollection of the Microsoft compiler was that it was indeed
MicroFocus but with a few oddities hung on or imposed by Microsoft.  I
can't recall anything specific except that it worked but it didn't
seem to measure up to the other offerings at the time.

My timeline may be off a bit, but Bill Klein noted in a post in
another thread (referring to MicroFocus) that, "Therefore, with the
slowing revenue stream along with several "poor" (if not disastrous)
product releases, there were frequent (at least annual) "management
RE-organizations".  My recollection of that period is consistent with
his.  MicroFocus went through some difficult times a decade or so ago,
which it seems to have overcome.

But that's all beside the point.  Regardless, for whatever reasons,
Microsoft lost interest in COBOL.  They didn't market it well when
they did.  Too bad, as it would be a different world if they had.

Kevin
Kevin J. Hansen
GUI ScreenIO - Windows(tm) Programming in COBOL!
http://www.ScreenIO.com
```
